Tales of the Riverne.
BY PRICE WARUNG.
Jim the Rebater.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
On the UppCr Murray.
The snow water is rushing swiftly between the
eucalypt-bordered banks, and on the fretted and
billowy surface of the green water float boughs
and branches that drank their primal sap from the
ice-cold heights .of the Snowys. Nestling in a
fork of a sapling are some sprigs of a blooming
acacia whose roots are in the Tumberumba
country ; a tortuous creeper, torn by a storm-blast
from the valley of the Eucumbene, clings to a
trunk which was nourished hard by Kiandra. Like
a big-sized bully into a throng of school lads
comes crashing through the swirl of the current
and the lighter timber, a monstrous red-gum that
the water has just wrested from the clayey
banks of the greatest of Australian rivers. The
tangle goes down stream together to communi-
cate in the quaint language of Nature its
message from lofty snow-rimmed peaks to plains
which burst arid split under the brazen sky, and
to tell the poor human who toils and sweats
amidst dust and aridity that, after all, there
is a joy and a glory in the world which
the great Riverine squatter, who tops the market
with his wool, cannot purchase with his wealth.
And with the tangle and the stream goes—a soul.
The red-gum log will forge its way through
r.-ach and bend, and will evade the grappling
rope as the sawmillers try, one after another, to
lasw it as it is hurled by their craft. It will
voy igc so for miles, and then it will
be cooped by a snag, which will hold it tight and
still tighter, till the suction of the under-tow draws
it do .11 and down, and embeds it in the bottom
drift, and it becomes a snag in its turn —the
f talest of tools to rip up planks and open seams,
ui d to dc. 'roy i i one desperate instant the fruit
of laborious yeai s.
And the sou l , too, goes down stream with the
mass of wood stuff, and after its journey of a few
hundred miles, it, tqo, falls a victim to the vicious
na 1 rtow of life, and becomes in its turn a snag,
which shall wreck another soul.
# # *
Jim Filzgerald came of a second genera-
tion of “gully-rakers.” The grandfather had
been one of the assigned; hands of a Monaro
pioneer, and, by the time he had won his freedom,
ha 1 githered a tidy little herd together—a
head here, and a head there, a “clear skin”
of his master’s now, and a Umeralla “wild
’un” then. He put his brand on them, and,
with a block of “out country” and an occa-
sional addition of a “stolen and strayed ” bull or
cow, (lid well. The ex-convict’s son took con-
genially to,the business. The father had known
some restraints of conscience, but had been careful
to give his boy a training which had dulled in his
earliest youth any tendency towards straightfor-
wardness in dealing with others’ goods. A course
of instruction which consisted almost entirely in
scouring gully and plain in order to stock a moun-
tain fastness at a neighbour’s expense was not
likely to induce a delicacy of discrimination
between what belonged to him of other species of
property and what did not. And nobody was
surprised when, after an excursion to Boyd Town
on Twofold Bay with cattle, he did not return
promptly. A sentence of twenty years on the
roads for horse-stealing interfered with his engage-
ments—and no living soul regretted his fate.
He had a wife and a boy of five when his “ mis-
fortune ” happened. The boy was too young to
understand, and the wife—well, she thanked God
for the crowning mercy of her husband’s arrest.
She had the habit of consulting Providence on all
occasions, for she had “found Christ.” When a
young woman who had just left school at Sydney,
she had heard in the old York-street Chapel a
sermon of the red-hot Methodist stamp. The
discourse was warm with the old-fashioned hell,
and splendid with particular details of the old-
fashioned heaven, and instinct with a passionate
yearning over a doomed race, fierce with an
intense horror of sin, gluttonous of conversions,
mad with the madness of the Hebrew prophets over
the stiff-necked and the perverse. No wonder the
girl, whose people had sent her to school because
her home-life was not likely to make her “ a
lady,” became “convinced of sin.” She knew
nothing of life, but of sin she knew (so she fancied)
much. And then, after one of those experiences
of storm and struggle which are all indispens-
able conditions of growth, so the old-style
Methodists thought, to the new soul, she “ found
peace.”
When she married, inveigled, she scarcely knew
how, by the dashing young cattle-dealer who came
once or twice a quarter with stock to Boyd Town
where her people kept store, she did not lose her
faith. On the contrary, it deepened and strength-
ened. Every fresh revelation of her husband’s
wrong-doing was a stab welcomed to the martyr’s
heart. Only, when the child came, she craved
that the Lord would help her to save the boy
from his father —perhaps for the church. And
in the father’s arrest she saw the hand of God.
By the time news of his conviction reached her in
the Fitzgerald homestead in a Cooma valley, she
had made up her mind what to do. Some
few head of stock she had brought with her upon
her marriage. These and their increase she sold.
And then, first bidding the police do their will
on the rest of the stock on their run, she took her
child and her money and disappeared from the
knowledge of the district. The child was hers
and the money she took was hers —and, as to the
future, was not the God of Sarah and Rebekah
and Mary her God ? He surely would save the
lad’s soul as He had saved hers.
And now, seventeen years after, the child of
five, grown into the young man of twenty-two,
stands upon the river bank at Corowa, and
watches the struggle of the brush and timber with
the stream. He has settled everything with h : s
dead mother’s lawyer, and has just been handed,
in the form of a bank draft on Melbourne, his
fortune. Not many hundreds—only five or six ;
and yet a fortune to the lad who has never left
the riverside, and to whom the cities are places
unknown, save for the one dread fact—“the
bad men lived there.” “Mrs. Fitzgerald’s Jim ”
had been known to all the country-side and river-
side people, and while many quietly laughed at,
all, at least apparently, deferred to her method of
training him. _ A storekeeper only, though she
might have coined wealth by joining a drink-
shanty to the store, she had always paid her way.
To do that and to bring Jim “ to A, sense of sin ’’
were her two ambitions. One other desire had
she—to keep from her boy the knowledge of his
father !
Men who did not hesitate to swindle her, yet
respected her wishes as regards Jim. Not for the
youngster’s sake, but for hers, they stripped their
blasphemy of its ornate blooms, and refrained
from telling yarns which are unprintable, and
singing songs that pollute the ear. They would
not teach him euchre, nor induct him into the
comparatively innocuous mysteries of “fifteen-
two, fifteen-four.” They even reduced, when in
his presence, the length of their snakes, and the
amount they had lost when Dan Morgan had
stuck them up. * They spoke of Jim among them-
selves as “Jim, the Vargin.” To his mother,
he was the child of many prayers, and as she
could not be blind or deaf to the difference in the
way the bullockies and bushies generally treated
her lad, and the way they treated others, she felt
the prayers would be answered sooner or later.
Were the rough fellows kept from tempting her
Jim, that was God—her God ! And surely, though
the signs were as yet uncertain, God’s mercy was
following, and He would be moved by her strenu-
ous stragglings to give unto her boy both the
“conviction of sin” and the grace that sustaineth
under the conviction, and that drives the penitent
to Christ.
And so, while Jim whistled in his care-free
youth, his mother wrestled in her agony for his
salvation.
# # *
When she was dying, her faith was un-
dimmed. She murmured that charter-song of the
Methodist who has “found peace
O Happy Day, that fixed my choice,
and pleaded that the Lord would bless her at the
last with the knowledge that Jim, too, “ could
read his title clear.” And Jim, wrought on by
his grief and his desire to grant unto her the one
consolation she craved, fancied, as so many others
have done, that the emotions of his impressionable
nature were the stirrings of the Spirit, and said
words to her and the Wesleyan minister who had
come from Corowa to her bedside that mads her
completely happy.
Waiting at the river-bank at Corowa to be
picked up by Mackintosh’s boat going down
stream with huge logs lashed fore and aft on
her hulking barge, h s fingered mechanically the
envelope in his pocket containing the draft. He
took it out and looked at it. From the folds,
dropped a bit of cardl oird. It was the “Society
Class-ticket of the people called Methodists.”
They had lost no time in enrolling him, and as he
looked at it, he recalled the whispered appeal
which had accompanied its issue to him “ Jimsey,”
said the class-leader, an old South-country man,
“ thee be motherless, now, lad, an’ I know ns wt o’
yer airthly father, but ye ’ast a Heavt nly one,
Jimsey —don’t never forgit that. An’ stick to th’
Class, lad—it’ll ’elp your soul mighty w’erever y’
be.” •
The words came back to him, borne, so it
seemed, on the rush of the current. They re-
minded him of his loss, and strangely drove in
upon his mind in a manner quite foreign,
to his ordinary experience that he had never
known his father. Father and mother and sister
and brother had the one parent he had known
been to him, and somehow, with that easy
acquiescence in things as they are which is so
characteristic of youth, he had never troubled
himself to specially inquire as to his father. The
district gossip allusively referred to Mrs. Fitz-
gerald as “ the widow ” as often as by her own
name, and he had taken the implication for a
granted fact. But, now, with the new routings in
his being of thought and reflection he pondered
over this gap in his personal history. Was hi?
father dead ? Then was it not singular that hi?
mother should not have spoken of him and hi?
resting-place?
Suppose he was not dead, but alive? Suppose,
further—the young man was gaining some insight
into life, and was becoming familiar with some of
the more vulgar aspects of family relationships—
that his father had abandoned his mother? Would
he, in that case, ever meet him ?
With the fervency of his new-born enthusiasm
for religious practices breathing upon him, what
more natural than that he should then and there
pray that he should meet his father, if his father
were still in the flesh? And he knew, in the
curious reflex action of spiritual exaltation, that
his prayer would be answered.
# * •
Carpet-bag in hand he sprang lightly
from the bank to the dingy which rowed in
shore for him. The dingy, on returning to the
steamer, was steered round the surging mass of
river drift-wood. Young Jim, still boyish in his
ways, dropped his disengaged hand into the water,
against the current. A tiny branch of the acacia,
laden with golden snow, the fluffy globules glisten-
ing dewily in their bath, was swept against his
fingers. He grasped it, and scarcely knowing
why he did so, withdrew it from the stream. As
he sprang on the decking of the steamer his im-
pulse was to throw the wattle back to the water.
But the hand of fate arrested the movement, and
a thought, new to him in its suggestiveness of
sentiment, arose.
Why should he not keep it as a memento of his
home-country? It would dry and shrivel, no
doubt, but it would still speak to him of the
Upper Murray country wherever he went. So
he retained it.
# * *
“ Now, my young cock’rel,” exclaimed the
mate, as he came up to the new passenger, ticket-
book in hand—Jimmy Macintosh, of the Echuca
sawmills, cannily Scotch in his ways, was careful
to demand vouchers for every trifling transaction
of his multifarious business —“ wot’s your name,
an’ w’ere ’re yer for ?”
“ Echuca,” answered the youth, “and James
Fitzgerald is my name.”
“ Oh, oh ! Jim Fitzgerald is it? an’ who’s yer
dad ?”
“ My father —but what’s that got to do with
you?”
“ Oh, nuthin’,” answered the brawny timber-
jigger, “ on’y our bargeman is named Jim Fitz-
gerald —an’ I b’leeve he comes from somew’eres
up this way.”
“My father is dead,” answered the lad, and
paying his sovereign for his fare, took the ticket
and turned away.
• • #
Timber-getting on the Murray, even when
varied by a little judicious evasion of the forest
regulations and a quiet bit of smuggling—not all
the tobacco smoked in Riverina paid border, or,
indeed, any duty—was a somewhat monotonous
life, and the steamer mate was but one of many
wtio would have seized upon such a trifle as the
coincidence of names of his bargeman and his
passenger to spin upon it jokes as to their sup-
posititious relationship. At the mid-day spell,
when a rouseabout relieved bargeman Fitzgerald

at the wheel of the barge,, so that he might go •
aboard the steamer for his snack, the mate was
fertile with chat—more fertile than delicate.
“ Hello, Jim ! ’ave you seen that young shaver
we’re takin’ down —the chap we picked up at
Corowa?” T , , ■
“No,” growled the bargeman, ‘an 1 don fc“
want to, neither.” - .
“Wal,” joked the other, shaking himself with
laughter, “that’s bad now, that is, consid rin as
he’s yer son.”
Fitzgerald, tall and lathy for all his year*, thin-
flanked, bushy-bearded, with eyes that _strove in
vain to look straight at men and things, was
pierced by the arrow shot at random.
“ What the do ye mean ?” he sharply said,
starting from the seat.
“Oh, nuthin’,” said the other—“on y yer go
and jest ask the chap—he ses as he s yer son !
Now, Fitzgerald the younger had said nothing
of the kind, but the humorous mate thought it an
element of fun to be unveracious. Strange that,
he should have thought so, for he had not culti-
vated his sense of humour in the city club smok-
ing-rooms, where they discuss reputations with a
regard for wit as pronounced as their disregard-
for truth !
With a repeated oath, the bargeman swung outc
of the cabin. “ He’d see, he would, what the
young blag’ard meant by sayin as he was
his son! ” As though the relationship when
proved would be a disgrace to him—him, the?
erstwhile gully-raker, ex-Cockatoo Island felon*
and present timber-getter.
The elder found the younger sitting-aft, gazings
at the rolling, swaying barge and wondering, in
the introspective fashion of youth, where this?
vovage of his was to lead him.
“I say, younker,” demanded the bargeman,.
“Who are you? An’ what d’ye mean by saying;
as you’re ray son ?” . , „
Shaken out of his reverie, the young fellow-
looked up wonderingly. . ,
•»I—I —said nothing of the sort! My father s
dead T And then a filmy reminiscence floated
like a vaporous cloud just within the horizon of
his experience, and through it loomed indistinctly ;
and undefined a bushy-bearded face, and hands•
that used to toss him to a horse’s mane.
“At least,” be stammered, “I always under- 1
stood so.” , . , ,
Face to face with the youth, the father knew
his son. There was the outline of his wife’s
features ;: something in his eyes of that clearness
of vision which had never failed to look straight
at things and at men, save when her husband had.
shamed her and when she bore vicariously his
disgrace. These he saw. Moreover, from the
heart of one the impalpable, irresistible shafts-
forged of kinship went to the heart of the other.
The father knew his son, the son knew his
father, and accepted the recognition as the answer
to his prayer. Such curious survivals does Nature
give us, first blurring or crossing them. The
mother had craved that her son might lean on
prayer as on a rock. The strain of her intense-
faith came up so far in him —and lo ! it had
worked to the destruction of her other great hope,
that he might never know his father. Of such
paradoxes is Life full.
# * *
Had there been in the minds of either the
faintest doubt as to the identity of the other, the
one feeble link needed to complete the chain oi
evidence would have been supplied by the branch
of dripping acacia which the yolinger Fitzgerald
held in his hand.
The father caught it, and the lore of the bush,
which seems to implant itself unconsciously in the
natures of men who are careless of its value,
■ prompting him to the enquiry : “ Where did you
get that wattle ?”
Not now the half-impertinent retort of youth ;
“ What business is that of yours?”—but the bare
truth.
“ I picked that up an hour ago.”
“ Did you? Why that is Turaberumba wattle !
/You can’t get that in these parts.”
“ I picked it out of the river as I was coming
-aboard.”
“Oh,” said the father, with an accent of dis-
appointment, “you didn’t pick it up near the
‘Tooma Creek?”
“ No,” said the other, “ just as I told you —but
it could have come down stream, you know.
Where is the Tooma? Doesn’t the Tooma run
into the Murray?”
“Why, that’s where you was born—between
- the Tooma and Tumberumba ! ”
Again the enquiry which yet to the questioner
seemed so unnecessary. “ How do you know
sfthat?”
For answer, “What was your mother’s name?”
- “Emma!” ,
The father received the shook, outwardly
'I unmoved. Did he not know it ?
“ Was she good—religious?”
• “She was a Wesleyan, and they thought a good
If lot of her.”
The words were convincing. _“ Come to the
Charge, I must have more talk with you,” said the
I elder.
% The steamer slackened a second to allow the
’A barge to draw up, and to be fended off when
father and son had leapt on its for’ard cargo.
’-‘They pursued their conversation, which meant so
to both, so fatally much to one, on the
wheel-platform.
The talk revealed many things to the man as
■fwoll as to the youth. He told his son how he had
; (.gone gold-digging, how., after years of failure,
had come in part, and how then he had
sought his home, but, to his dismay, found his
A wife and child missing. And the young Jim
swallowed the lie. “ I ain’t a-denyin’ it, my lad ”
—the rogue forced a paternal kindness into the
words —“I ain’t been as good a chap as I ought
to a’ been. I wasn’t never good nuff for your
mother.” Affecting repentance now, and sobriety
of behaviour and sppech, he did not jar upon the
lad, and won his liking insensibly.
* * •
Now, there was in existence at this epoch
of Riverine trading a practice which robbed the
Victorian railways of no inconsiderable portion of
their revenue. The system of making rebates or
refunds of freight on all goods carried over the
lines for destinations beyond the Wakool Junc-
tion, in order to attract the Lower Riverina trade
to Melbourne, supplied the reason for this prac-
tice—and the profit of it. The method of the
fraud need not bo told at the time of present
writing. Suffice it to say that big men made big
money by it. Some small men were ambitious of
doing the same. One of these latter was bargeman
Fitzgerald. When his son handed him undoubt-
ingly the bank-draft on Melbourne, he saw a way
to do it. He bought a share in a boat.
A railway clerk, a shipping agent’s clerk, and
at least a mate or bargeman—these were the tool's
necessary to the fraud. Fitzgerald himself could
look after*the boat’s share of the swindle; he had
the shipping agent’s clerk “readied up;” he
wanted but one more accessory : the railway clerk.
And here, in his son, he found that desideratum,
for it was an easy matter in those days for a river-
man well-known in Echuca to obtain for his son
a billet in Customs or railway. And Jim, junior,
had been not indifferently educated, and was soon
well up to the routine of cheeking way-bills and
freight invoices. In two months he was rebate
clerk —expert in checking and passing the sheets
on which shipping agents and boat-owners pre-
sented their claims for refunds. To distinguish
him from his father, who was-“ Jim Fitzgerald,
the Bargeman,” colloquially Jim,, junior, became
“Jim, the Rebater.” And the bargeman’s plans
throve right prosperously.
# * *
For some months they throve. And then
the mocking devil which lives in the core of every
ill-deed and forces it, from sheer derision, into the
light of day to become a thing abhorrent, prompted
Jim the Bargeman to reveal unto Jim the
Rebater a scheme for an unusually large haul of
fraudulent rebate vouchers. Then the boy, so far
an innocent tool—doing things in routine fashion
because others had done so and his superior officers
had told him to do it—awoke to the criminal
nature of his doings. He refused.
Several times he was pressed, but refused
always—swore, moreover, to inform the Station-
master of the roguery that went on under his nose
were he asked once more.
And .there was, the Bargeman thought, that
mother’s strain in his son’s blood which would
have made him carry out his threat in scorn of
consequences. So he would not press him further.
* » *
The great mills were busy. Night and
day were the giant saws going, tearing into beams
and planks and battens the spoil of New South
Wales forests, and shivering into the timber of com-
merce monster red gums with a breaking-weight of
lOjOOOlbs. to the square inch. And The boats were
busy, too, unloading the raw logs, loading with
the milled material. Jim’s barge lay under the
shoots up which the huge boles were chain-hauled
to slide down similar ways tobhe saw-beds. Jim
was guiding the chains one night when his boy
came down. The night was gloomy, and the
work was proceeding in the glare of big reflector
lamps which threw here broad splashes of bril-
liancy and there shadows as of midnight.
“ Good nig'ht, father ! 1 heard you were in and
came down to ”
“Well—what?” The Bargeman felt pretences
need scarcely be kept up longer.
“To say that I must tell Mr. Francis ’’—Francis,
now Acting-Commissioner, was stationmaster
then —“ of these rebate tricks.”
“ You’ve got vartuous all of a sudden.
Who’s put you up to this now?”
“ My mother’s memory !”
“Hell! ’Tis always your cantin’ hypocrite of
a mother. You’re gettin’ as big a sneak as she
was ! ”
“ My mother was a good woman —and I’m sorry
I can’t be as good. But I’ve been to Class ”
“What?” roared the father. “ Methody
Class ?”
“Yes!”
“ Damn you ! That means as you mean to give
me ’wajq do you ?”
* # #
The head of the shoots was in. a sheet of
darkness. Who can say what happened ? A slip
either sidq would precipitate the stumbler. On
the river-side, he could receive no injury but a
shaking. On the other
Jim the Rebater tripped—or was thrown. Thp
guiding chains slacked, but it was not the great
descending trunk that delivered the fatal blow.
The saw caught him first.
Devil of Truth.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Devil of Truth ! for Angel thou art not.
Falsehood’s the Angel, tEou Devil that hates
Man !
Tell me, O Devil ! what angels impart not;
Cant not of Honour —what of the Statesman?
“ Boodle.”
Devil of Truth ! Man carves him his Idol,
Sceptred and throned with a right to determine;
Bows toJiis fiat a neck suicidal;
Cant not of Justice —what of the Ermine?
“ Pander.”
Devil of Truth ! Man yields to his Rulers,
Regiments enforcing commands of each sireling,
Butchering swift all the rebels and foolers ;
Cant not of Glory—what of the Hireling ?
“ Loot.”
Devil of Truth ! Man has his soul-leaders,
Fuming with phosphoric vapours from Full Pit,
Filing men’s hearts with the passion of pleaders;
Cant pot of Purity—what of the Pulpit?
“ Collections .”
Devil of Truth ! (Ware wrath ! This is my lot).
Parsons may pander till Hell is their creditor,
Man has, besides them, a guide and a pilot;
Cant not of Duty—what of the Editor ?
“ Advertisements
Devil of Truth ! take Commerce that gives Man
Broad news of the Nations, and Knowledge
that aids Man—
Banking and brewing and everyhow lives Man;
Cant not of Honesty—what of the Tradesman?
“ Cash doivn! ”
Devil of Truth ! What millions of Workers,
Womb-launched, grave-bound, the bond-slaves
of the Classes,
Swim in their sweat, the mere prey of the lurkers!
Cant not of Manhood —what of the Masses !
“ Fools always.” ')
Devil of Truth !. . . “ Stay ! My turn to ques-
tion.
Money’s Man’s God ! ’Tis the Bin the Billion
What’s that best man-gift that best men will best
shun
Till he who has it is One in a Million?’’

SATURDAY, JANUARY T, 1893.
The Kanaka Paradise—By Bull’s
Daughter.
The London Times, which lately sent a special
lady correspondent to Australia to “consider”
the Kanaka slave-trade, has published the first
results of its representative’s mission. Her
decision, and that of the Times, as summarised
per cablegram, is that the Kanaka trade is
“ above the shadow of reproach,” and that
North Queensland is a Paradise for the South
Sea islander, a species of Promised Land where
the good Polynesian goes—a field of Asphodel
where a beatific nigger soars to higher and
better,,things while hoeing cane in an ideal cane-
brake for a boss who is but one remove from an
angel. As far as can be gathered from the
cabled summary, the Kanaka trade is altogether
too fair and spotless a thing for this sin-stained
earth. “Many of the plantations possess
schools, and nearly all of them have regular
concerts and sports.” The writer “never saw a
dejected Kanaka,” nor saw, nor heard of, any
instance in which a Polynesian “ was subjected
to drudgery.” And—here the correspondent
strikes the climax of eulogy—she has never
“seen the average white labourer so well off or
so comfortable. V\ hich, considering the state
of the average white labourer as seen in Eng-
land, sounds like an enthusiastic oongi atu.atiou
tendered to a lost soul in the third hell because he
isn t fried on quite so fierce a fire, and has
a better and more commodious gridiron tluiu
the lost soul of the seventh. Ihe toiler whose
state is only a little better than that of tue
British agricultiuist, is not a being to be envied,
and when that forlorn wretch is dragged in as a
means of comparison the presumption is that
hem the only individual that the writer can
think of whose condition is more wretched than
that of the recruited Kanaka M hen any-
body is congratulated on the fact that he is
bettor oil than ho would be m perdition, it
naturally suggests that perdition is the only
place where he could be worse off. Ihe brutal
Hodge, bond-slave of the squire and the Church
by Laxv Established, isn t actually in perdition,
but the life he leads is a \ ery fair substitute.
, * , ,
ine fact that the cable-agent Ims taken the
trouble to forward, and that the capitalistic
press of Australia has taken the trouble to
print, the views of the Times on this question,
plainly imply that these views are supposed to
be something of considerable weight. If the
Times’ opinion doesn’t actually settle the
whole problem it goes a long way towards
toing so. The Englishman, and the kind of
Australian who is even more English than the
Englishman, regards that massive and long-
discredited publication, the organ of Sampson’s
mendacities, and the poor tool of forger I’ioott,
as the final authority on all subjects under
Heaven. Yet the Times, after all, is only a
heavy country squire named Walter, and Co.,
and the views of the paper are only the views of
Walter and the other man or two. Neither
Walter nor the other man has ever, so far as
the world is aware, been either in Australia or
Polynesia. Neither of them, in all probability,
ever saw a Kanaka, or a Queensland sugar-
planter, or a canefleld, or a recruiting-agent, or
a South Sea slaver, or any other adjunct of the
unholy, bloodstained trade. Their correspon-
dent, who declares the recruiting business to
be without any shadow of reproach, has been
in Australia perhaps six, perhaps eight
weeks, perhaps even three months, and
has never visited the islands, nor seen the
recruiting industry carried on, nor sailed in a
slave ship, nor learned, except at second hand,
by the reports of interested parties, anything
whatever about the nefarious traffic. She could
speak none of the native dialects, and, there-
fore, her reports necessarily represent one side
of the case only. But she was duly accredited
to Samlkl Griffith (the man who, in other
days, denounced the South Sea slave-trade as
forever accursed, and gave reasons for his
denunciation which are as good as new
to-day), and asked him if the trade which he
had re-created was in any way objection-
aide, and if he was really the author of a
system of murder, piracy and wholesale kidnap-
ping, whereupon he enthusiastically responded
in the negative. Also, she visited sundry show-
plantations as an honoured guest—which was
practically the same as the accused standing
champagne for the Judge—when everything
was prepared for her reception, and the diseased
nigger was, of course, removed out of sight, and
the bruised nigger was put away in the back-
ground (for who would be so rude as to show
such sights to a lady ?), and the sound and pre-
sentable nigger had been instructed to smile for
his life, and wear all the look of profound happi-
ness that he could muster. And out of such
materials is constructed that unbiassed public
opinion which the daily press lays on in every
household as if it were a public water-supply
or a gas-service.
# « *
The Times, in its anxiety to whitewash the
capitalists of Queensland and that great Im-
perialist statesman, Sir Samuel Griffith,
K.C.M.G., has convicted itself out of its
own mouth. There is not—that is the
assertion in the cablegram—in North Queens-
land, among all the Polynesians whom the
Times representative passed in review, even
one dejected face. And following this comes
the statement that the Kanaka’s condition is
“ better than that of the English agricultural
labourer.” Truly, if the first statement is true
then the Kanaka’s condition is better than that
of the British House of Lords, better than that
of all the monarchs of Europe, better by far
than that of all the statesmen and millionaires
of this earth, and only about one shade worse
than that of. the angels. And if only the second
assertion is true, then the islander is just one
remove, and no more, from Gehenna. The dis-
crepancy between the two ends of the descrip-
tion is altogether too awful. The Kanaka is
happier, so far as outward appearances go, than
it is given to any mere human being to be on
this earth—also, he is better off than if he were
being killed by starvation and slow, hopeless,
incurable, long-drawn-out misery. He is more
joyful than any man or woman on record out-
side Elysium—also, it is the general opinion
that he is more comfortable than if he was
being grilled over a slow fire. It is a lame
and unspeakably impotent conclusion, and
the method by which it was arrived at is the
same by which the Times arrived at many
similar conclusions in the past. By an almost
identical process that paper, when it sought as
the mouthpiece of English capitalism to blast
out the foundations of the great Republic,
decided that the blood-boltered African slave
of the Confederate States was an ideal nigger
leading a butterfly existence among the mag-
nolias—a joyous child of nature singing and
dancing gaily in a Paradise that was located
somewhere on the dreary, clay banks of the Red
River. It resolved in the same way —viz., by
taking the unbiassed opinion of the parson and
the squire—that the English Hodge is and
always has been a contented and prosperous
peasant ; and it has learned—from the absentee
landlord and the rack-renting agent—that the
Irish agriculturist is a being absolutely without
grievances of any kind whatsoever. When the
Times starts out to explain away a great wrong
the results can be compared to no literary pro-
duction on the face of the earth—save, per-
haps, the prospectus of the late Mr. Walk ford
Sqeers’ academy.
* # %
If the “great” English journal desires to pene-
trate the mysteries of the Kanaka traffic, it must
proceed on an entirely differentsystem. It should
first catch a lady absolutely destitute of precon-
ceived opinions and have her taught the liquid
dialect of some of the South Sea archipelagoes.
Then she should be painted black in enduring
colours, and clothed chiefly in a disused sack,
and be recruited in due form so that she might
be inducted into the inner life of the Kanaka in
happy Queensland. And after that she could
study at leisure the method of recruiting, and the
manner in which the recruit is sold by his
alleged friends and relatives and chiefs to
Griffith’s slave-dealers ; the morality of cap-
tains, mates, and Government agents; the
system under which the island women are
not recruited unless accompanied by their
husbands ; the relations between captain
and agent, the extent to which the latter
acts as a check upon the former, and the
reasons for believing that he isn’t paid a com-
mission in consideration of a judicious silence
upon many subjects (as was invariably the case
in the good old days) ; the manner in which an
inspector, who doesn’t know the native lan-
guage, and an interpreter, who is often equally
ignorant, assure themselves that all is honest
and above-board ; the joys of life on a labour
schooner, also the victuals, the odours, the dis-
cipline, the orgies carried on by tltfc Officers,
and the manner in which all hands conduct
themselves towards the good-looking Polynesian
maiden when they get liei' on board. Further’
she would gather much valuable information
about plantation life, about the drunks, the
lights, the doings of the suave boss and over-
seer when they are not being inspected by an
influential lady visitor, the food, the hospital
system, the consumptives who are said to work in
the sugar-houses when they are too dilapidated
for further labour in the cane-fields, the reasons
why there are on an average about two female
to a hundred and twenty male recruits
(that was the proportion in the very last
slaver which arrived at Bundaberg), and the
physical and moral consequences thereof, the
causes whereby the Kanaka —said to be specially
adapted to tropical agriculture—dies off (as per
Queensland official statistics, mind you) from
three to seven times as fast as the white inhabit-
ants—who are supposed not to be similarly
adapted ; the curious absence of any effective
inquiry into this tremendous mortality ; and
many kindred topics. And when the inquiry
is finished, and the Times has rescued its bruised
and damaged correspondent from her “master,”
that paper will be able, for the first time, to
speak on the Kanaka question as one having
authority.
# # #
But even if, after all this investigation, it is
demonstrated that the Polynesian is supremely
content with his lot, and that hoeing cane at
€6 per annum is a foretaste of Paradise, it
makes absolutely no difference in the situation.
The Times may paint the slave-trader and gild
the refined Kanaka till the end of history, but
it thereby merely evades the real issue. The
islander may be happy in Queensland, just as
the Jew sweater may be happy in England, and
the Russian invader, if he ever arrives, may be
happy in London when he is looting that golden
city, and as the leper may be quite content any-
where, but Queensland was not built expressly
as a haven of refuge for any cheap foreign
labourer, whatever the English press, which
regards our country as England’s private pro-
perty and a mere stamping-ground for exploita-
tion, may think to the contrary. If the Times,
or any other foreign paper, is prepared to
demonstrate that the white labourer in Queens-
land is contented, well-fed, and prosperous,
that his lot in that Kanaka-ridden land is
“above the shadow of reproach,” and that
there is no dejected white man, as well as no
dejected Polynesian, to be found in the land,
then the question may be regarded as settled.
If it is not prepared to prove this, then even
though the South Sea islander should die of joy
at Bundaberg, the trade is just about as
nefarious as it was before in the days when the
rum-soaked Queensland pirates of the Hopeful
let out the life-blood of Johnny Malk oeo on
the high seas.
The Modern Bastille.
At first sight, it looks quite disinterested
for the New South. Waks “vested interest”
party to object to the abolition of imprisonment
for debt, for it is up to the neck in debt itself.
But the fat man’s debt is not necessarily the
sort of debt which involves imprisonment under
the local'law. A writ of c«. so. doesn’t touch
the church deacon who has borrowed the
washerwoman’s savings at eight per cent on a
promissory-note that will be dishonoured at
maturity. He is all right. If she is reasonable,
and makes no fuss, he will square it for seven-
and-six in the £. If she is cantankerous and
takes out a writ, then he delays her for six
months by filing a lying defence. At the end of
that time, supposing she has not come to grief
on a legal snag and gets her judgment, he files
his schedule. His bogus creditors roll up, and
vote that he be allowed his furniture, including
the grand piano and the walnut dining-room
suite bought with the washerwoman’s money,
and she gets a dividend of one and a penny-
farthing. He doesn’t go to gaol. That is not
what is meant in New South Wales by im-
prisonment for debt.
* # •
But if the washerwoman throws the thirteen-
pence-farthing at the deacon’s head, and tells
him the truth about himself—that he is a flat-
footed swindler, then the law of imprisonment
for debt at once begins to operate. She is sued
for slander, and the deacon employs two eiftinent
Q.C.’s and an assorted variety of other lawyers.
The Judge directs the jury to find for plaintiff;
and when they assess the damage to his char-
acter at a farthing, certifies for costs, amount-
ing to £1392 4s. Bd. Whereupon the woman
has to go to gaol for twelve months, unless she
can pay that sum—which she can’t—and bank-
ruptcy won’t help her to get out of gaol; nor
will it ever enable her to get clear of the
liability after she has served her year in prison.
That, in brief, is the New South Wales law of
imprisonment for debt. It is a law to imprison
every man who unsuccessfully defends a
defamation action, unless he has money. It
doesn’t touch the man who incurs a debt that
he never means to pay and knows he can’t pay.
W hich is the reason that the fat man approves
of it.
* * #
It is a mistake to suppose that the repeal of
this law would take away the only means of
punishing the penniless defamer, who recklessly
circulates malicious slanders. The lawyer who
says that that would bo the effect of the repeal
is either lying or is ignorant of the law. The
Bixlktin does not and never did advocate any
law to allow false and wanton libels to be pub-
lished with impunity. It is possible to injure a
man just as cruelly by attacking his character
as by knocking him down and jumping on his
stomach ; and the miscreant who" makes the
one attack should be liable to be dealt with in
just the same way as the ruffian who makes the
other. The criminal law accordingly provides
exactly the same means of dealing with both of
them. The individual who has been assailed,
cither in character or-in person, can lay an in-
formation against his assailant; and on the
charge being proved the law awards a punish-
ment supposed to be proportionate to the
gravity of the offence.
* # m
But this punishment is not intended as a
salve for the wounded feelings of the prosecutor,
or as a means of gratifying his desire for re-
venge. Our law does not recognise the right of
an injured man to retaliate on his aggressor,
r l hat is to say, it does not recognise such a right
except in the one instance of defamation. The
general principle is, ostensibly at least, that the
criminal law exists for the purpose of punishing
offences against the community, and offences
against individuals in so far only as they are
injurious to the community, with a view to dis-
couraging'the commission of such offences. The
injured individual’s right is confined, not to
retaliation, but to reparation. And that he
must seek through civil process. The two
things are sharply distinguished in principle,
and should be kept so in practice the
criminal law to punish the offence in the
interests of the community; the civil law to
award compensation for the injury done to the
individual. If a man have his health damaged
by a kick from his friend, he is entitled to
recover out of his friend’s property a sum
sufficient to make reparation for the damage.
But he is not entitled to glut his vengeance by
putting the other in gaol. That is a matter
solely within the province of society at large,
represented by the Attorney-General, or who-
ever the public prosecutor may, be. And, in
this instance, practice conforms to theory. The
plaintiff with the pounded stomach may obtain
a verdict for £lOOO damages, and if the defen-
dant’s property will produce that much under
execution, the plaintiff gets the money. But if
the defendant’s belongings do not amount to a
thousand farthings, the plaintiff is not allowed
to console himself for his disappointment by
thrusting his debtor into a dungeon and keeping
him there.
* # #
Why should there be any other system in the
case of a libel ? A libel may or may not be
a graver offence than an assault. It may be a
deliberate and abominable outrage ; it may be
a harmless slip of the pen requiring a great
strain to make it defamatory even in a technical
sense. If the offender is to be punished, the
punishment should be apportioned by a proper
tribunal after the same sort of enquiry as would
be held on any other criminal charge. We can
best show what is done by giving an instance.
The Bulletin has occasionally made mistakes
and has faced the music. Unwarrantable
statements get into all papers at times, and
when we have been clearly in the wrong we
have taken our gruel without a whimper. But
once in its early days The Bulletin defended
a libel-action, and the jury awarded the plain-
tiff a farthing damages. Had The Bulletin
proprietors been put on their trial criminally
for that libel, before a tribunal that took a
similar view, they would have been found guilty
and imprisoned for about ten seconds. Yet, under
the N.S. W. law, because the Judge certified for
costs that totalled up more than the proprietors
were worth, they were sent to gaol for a year.
They didn’t stay there for quite a year ; but that
was not the fault of the law. In point of fact,
they were fined a farthing for the libel, and
sentenced to twelve months imprisonment for
not being able to pay u lawyer’s bill.
* * #
On the other hand, the penniless adventure',
who brings a foundationless blackmailing action
against a newspaper runs no such risk if be
fails. He cannot be gaoled for not paying the
defendants’ lawyer’s bill. They have to pay
the piper themselves, and no law steps in to
give them the gratification of taking it out of
his hide. The law of so-called imprisonment
for debt is kept alive solely for the purpose of
enabling persons to be punished as criminals
for an offence of which no jury could find
them guilty if they were charged with it in the
criminal dock. It is a law by which a man can
be kept in gaol for a year for want of a five-
pound note. It is the apotheosis of the scanda-
lous fine -or - imprisonment system. “ Forty
shillings or a month ”is bad enough. It is a
sieve, adjusted to let the kid-gloved criminal
through every time, while it catches the in-
dividual that ‘ ‘ society ” objects to. “ A farthing
and costs, or twelve months,” ;s built on the
same lines, only more so. The Bulletin’s
views on this question sum up shortly thus :
The gaols should lie kept for criminals. Before
anyone, even a newspaper-man, is sent to gaol
as a criminal, he should be put on his trial, and
given the same privileges as a bank-robber or a
murderer; and, if found guilty, he should be
set to do his time on hominy whether he is a
gutter-snipe or a millionaire. Further, no man
in the country should be allowed to keep
another in prison to gratify his private spite.
Or, if it is to be allowed, let the thing be carried
out systematically, and give us a good, frank,
wholesome system of leftres de cachet’ and a new
Bastille.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Shiels's Latest.
Victoria seems mildly excited by the discovery
that Premier Shiels, the uncompromising
Patriot of Casterton, has worked another job
upon his adopted country. The job itself is
small and much less contemptible than the
effort made to prove Shiels -a man of unim-

Speaking of the Mercantile Bank and other
troubles in Melbourne, the local smileless
Punch sternly remarks :
It was not well done on the part of Jack Murray to
make that incriminatory speech of his in Parliament. It
is true that he voiced the general community ; but it was
a speech for the prosecution made in a place where no
defence could be offered. That is not a piece of British
fair play.
Which, when you follow it to its logical con-
clusion, just amounts to this : Nobody must
expose a foul wrong in Parliament unless the
doer of the wrong is there and in a position to
answer the charges. No one is allowed to
answer a charge in Parliament unless he
is a member of that honourable body.
Therefore when a piece of irredeemable
blackguardism is perpetrated by anybody
the perpetrator must straightway be elected a
member of the House before the Legislature
can move in the matter, and when all the con-
spicuous swindlers have been so elected there
will be no honest man in the House to expose
anything. They are fearfully and wonderfully
anxious to temper the wind to the shorn bogus
banker down in Melbourne. Yet, when Punch
publishes its weekly howl against trades-
unionists and their leaders it never stops to
consider that it is denouncing them in a place
where no defence can be offered. Further, the
sickening wheeze about “ British fair play ” is
being done to death. What’s the matter with
Australian fair play ? If there isn’t any fair-
play except the imported article it is time a
duty were put on it to encourage local pro-
duction.

any appreciable extent, because he probably
gets no more than when he was a lieutenant,
and perhaps he only got nothing at all then.
But to the mock soldier there must be some
extra glory in the rank of eaptain. Therefore,
as George Chirnside talked of abandon-
ing Hie job ot supporting a few cannons and
sufficient men to keep them loaded, and as the
Hopetouns honoured him with a visit and
exhorted him to stick to his blessed guns.
Lieutenant George gets his tin-pot promotion.
That’s all,.
Anarchy.
Editor Bulletin,—As some people know, I am
an anarchist. I was one of the first persons jn
Australia to join this movement, becoming a
member of the Melbourne Anarchists 5 Club in
188(i. The opinions promulgated at that time
differed, however, from my present views in the
fact that the application of the basic principle
(individual autonomy) was more limited. We
formulated, in our platform, the abolition of law
and the State, and also of rent, profit, and interest,
but adhered to the idea of wages, prices, and
ownership; and those who preferred communistic
methods to the rigid system of exchange at
“labour cost” favoured by the majority, did so
on the grounds of expediency or of vague senti-
ment. In 1888 I perceived that ownership (with-
out which there would be, strictly speaking,
neither wages nor prices) was simply a conven-
tional law or restriction, and modified my ideas
in accord with this discovery. Then, ascertaining
that the views as altered were held by the modern
Communist Anarchist party of Europe, I adopted
that distinguishing designation. Communism in
this sense, which agrees strictly with its literal in-
terpretation, is really synonymous with absolute
individualism, although the latter term is almost
monopolised by the upholders of Property. I was,
so far as I can ascertain, the first person in any
part of the world to demonstrate the essentiality
of Communism to Anarchy, and establish it
logically on an anarchistic basis.
* * *
What is Anarchy ? Well, in the first place, I
decline to acknowledge the claim of anyone else to
dictate to me what I shall or shall not do. My
body is constructed by Nature to move under the
impulsion of the brain and nerves which form part
of it, not of those which form part of somebody
else, and I do not propose to shut them up and
hitch on to anybody else’s, at any rate unless he
can do the feeling and suffering for me as well as
the choosing of .a course. And, were I to do so,
I should simply be committing moral suicide in
order to endow him with an additional body to
use (if lie were so disposed) in forcing others to
follow bis will rather than their own. In that
case I should be a standing menace to society, just
as a Gatling-gun would be, and it is highly painful
to me to conceive of myself as being so._ There-
fore, I decline to abnegate my self-will in favour
of anyone who insists on my conforming either to
a formulated command or to a custom. I am
myself, and I am going to be myself as emphati-
cally as possible.
* # •
In the second place, with special regard to
ownership (which is the conventional privilege
accorded to the owner of dictating to everybody
else whether and on what terms something may
be availed of) and its derivatives, viz., wages,
prices, and the whole superstructure of capitalism,
I make this observation. Here we have a race of
beings who, unlike all other living things, imagine
they have no right to avail themselves of anything
whatever, saving certain exceptions in favour of
individuals with respect to particular things, and
fully half their energies are employed in the one
task of shifting the incidence of the taboo.
Naturally, the inconvenience is such that each
tries to get as many objects, or as large a propor-
tion of them, as possible, into the position of not
being tabooed to him, but that is, under the
system, synonymous with prohibiting everyone
else from as much as possible, and thus the actual
utility of all objects, except some for strictly
personal consumption, is reduced to the minimum,
and the amount of labour required to achieve a
given result of utility increased to the maximum.
# * #
In a condition of Anarchy, I should devote
myself, in the first instance, to works directly
beneficial to myself, and when I was not doing
so, and had the energy to spare, I should expend
it in the labours that were most congenial to me
for the benefit of others, as occasion arose, with
no thought of demanding reward from them for so
doing, because I should know that anybody would
be ready to assist me just as freely when I re-
quired any aid —and similarly in respect of goods,
which would be available to anyone at need. Thus
brotherly hell fulness would take the place of
bargain-driving, and labours would be automati-
cally divided according to congeniality ; and it is
safe to say that people’s faculties would be more
evenly exercised, and that each would acquire a
wide experience and skill, instead of each having
one faculty jaded and the rest atrophied, as at
present.
J. A. ANDREWS
(A Leading Sydney Anarchist.)
To attain to this ideal it is only necessary that
those wh,o accept it should put it in practice
among themselves, and, as far as possible, also
towards those who have not yet accepted it, while
conscientiously maintaining their own autonomy
in the measure of their power to do so. Simple
friendship and good will are capable of enabling
the last almost as easily as the first of these
to be effected in the majority of instances,
but, in some cases, the attempt to ignore laws and
privileges will be met or followed by violence on
the part of the adherents of the old system. Then,
of course, force in opposition will haye to be used,
unless the defensive purpose can be equally well
effected by evasion. But as we cannot live ac-
cording to Anarchy and according to the present
system at the same time, the policy of the future,
as soon as the ideas are sufficiently rooted in the
people, will be in the main to cease looking to
employment or trading in any form for the means
of subsistence, and settle upon the lands without
asking leave or paying tribute, in small groups
having communistic relations internally and
among each other, and, as far as practicable,
limiting their transactions with the outer world
also to dealings of a communistic character, This,
carried on internationally, will constitute so large
a drain on the profit-yielding population as to
reduce commercial values to nil, and the acquired
resources of civilisation, left in temporary idle-
ness, can then be “jumped ” by those who need,
not to appropriate, but to use them. On account
of the same draining process the possibility of
sparing men from the dwindling party of the pre-
sent system, to attempt to re-institute it by force,
will be, as also that of maintaining them, con-
stantly less and less, so that at least the later
stages of the Revolution will be comparatively if
not wholly bloodless.
# # #
In conclusion, I believe from experience (and
mine has been harder and more ruled by fraud and
tyranny than most people’s) that the deepest in-
stinct implanted in the human breast is that ex-
pressed in the words “ Do unto others as ye would
have them do unto you,” and when the fetters of
law, conventionality, and religion are broken, it
will find its way into action. — J. A. Andrews.

A Stone.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was midnight in the claim as elsewhere. Ihe
stars were obscured, the wind was blowing
furiously. A large lantern burned irregularly
beside a short, broad - shouldered man, who
was guiding the nozzle. He was a miner, taking
advantage of the heavy rain—a common practice
on the diggings. , .
Occasionally a flash of lightning illumined the
scene. The iron piping swelled like varicose veins
under the awful strain. The increasing water
strained the rivets with the pressure. The
cylindrical tube seemed to breathe.
Suddenly it burst with a loud report. The iron
tore like a rag, and the water hurled the pro-
prietor into the tail-race. A wild yell, stifled at
its loudest moment, rang out across the camp.
We grasped our moleskins and dragged them
on. We slid into our boots, snatched a lantern,
and hurried to the spot. We found the unfortu-
nate owner in the centre of the slush. His eyes
and ears were full of sediment, so was his mouth.
We harried down with him, and wrapped him in
hot blankets. He threw up a quantity of sand
and water, and soon fell asleep. He was appar-
ently well in the morning.
But in a few days an unnatural pallor settled on
his face. Symptoms of jaundice appeared, and
the poor fellow presently expired. At the post-
mortem the cause of death was given as a stone in
the stomach. Now, that stone was the size of an
egg, and was of aqueous formation. It belonged
to the family of sedimentary rocks. It had been
swallowed during the fright.
How was it gulped down ? Under no ordin-
ary circumstances could such a lump have
been swallowed. Neither is gastric juice favour-
able to cement. It is, therefore, certain that when
the accident took place, the stone was thrown
into the mouth, the cold water contracted the
inner muscles of the throat, a wild, mad gulp of
fear caused a momentary expansion of the gullet,
and it had been forced down. For the time being
the man was a veritable stork, which bird has for
its throat a tube, resembling indiarubber in elas-
ticity. The apparently impossible may occur in
the human body.
Maoriland.
POLYPE.
The real Cause of the challenge sent by
Kingston, Q.C., to the portly and pompous Baker,
C.M.G., in Adelaide, was a suspicion that the
latter had something to do with the sudden depar-
ture, on a month’s leave, of Railway-Chairman
Smith. The Legislative Council lately appointed
a committee to enquire into certain special vvheat-
rates secretly made by the Railway
for the benefit, apparently, of a few milling firms
that desired to keep local prices down by import-
ing Victorian grain, and, by Chaffey Baker’s infill:
ence, it is averred, the report indirectly accused
Kingston, who was Acting-Premier when the rates
were agreed to, of telling a falsehood. At his re-
quest the Assembly appointed a Commission to
consider the report and evidence of the Council s
committee, and Smith, of course, would be an
important witness. Kingston arid Baker were
colleagues in Ministerial office eight years ago.
but since that date there has been deadly feud
between them, and during the last few days of the
just-expired session they have, from their vantage
spots in each House of Legislature, flung fierce-
toned defiance at each other. Baker called King-
ston a bully, a coward, and a disgrace to
the legal profession. Kingston accused Bak rof
being politically “a public defaulter,” a slanderer,
and various other unreliable and perfidious
things. For days Kingston brooded over
the accusation of cowardice, and at last,
the announcement of Smith’s departure being the
last straw, he sat down and wrote a challenge.
He had first been round the gunsmith’s shops to
find two revolvers exactly alike —one of these,
with a packet of cartridges, he enclosed in the
ietter to Baker, and put the other in his pocket.
The missive began thus: “So, Smith is going
away for a month, and you are trying to burke
the enquiry.” Then, after some very outspoken
criticisms, it went on to say that, as the
post - office chimed the half - hour after one
(on Friday, Dec. 23) he would emerge
from Victoria Square, just opposite Baker’s
office, while Baker was at the same time to leave
his own door. They were then to advance towards
each other across the street, the signal to com-
mence firing being when either stepped from the
footpath on to the street. They were to fire after-
wards as quickly and in whatever manner they
liked, “until one of us goes out.” The request
was made that no innocent third party should be
brought into the matter. Kingston kept his
engagement to the second, but, instead of
Baker, was confronted by four plain-clothes
policemen, brought to the spot by Baker’s
son, to whom the letter had been shown.
Baker alleges that he drove up from the
Adelaide Club to his office a minute before, the
time agreed upon with the revolver and cartridges
in his pocket ready to defend himself, but he must
have known that the officers were already waiting
to seize his opponent, who did not see him. Kings-
ton was in dead earnest, and when the writer said
to him, “ Well, what will be the upshot of this?”
he replied, “ I don’t know, but whatever it may
be I had prepared myself for worse.” When
asked whether he did not feel pretty sure that
information would be given to the police, he
answered: —“Well, no, I didn’t think Baker
was such a cur.” He had very
great provocation, which was all the more likely
to work upon him because of his blood—he is on
his father’s side Irish, and Spanish on his mother’s;
while, as a soldier, be could hardly brook being
called a coward. It would have been wiser, how-
ever, had he had recourse to the horsewhip or
fists. In this Baker might have acted as his
exemplar, for in 1867, when a vigorous youth, he
thrashed a parson-journalist who is stated to have
written that Hon. John Baker, C.M.G., father of
Chaffey, induced a number of black gins to dance
in a state of nudity before the Duke of Edinburgh.
Kingston and Baker are both big men and in pretty
good physical condition, and if the contest
could be'arranged between them in aid of the
Broken Hill relief fund, the proceeds ought to be
very large. Whatever the result of the bout,
there would be a grim satisfaction in making Baker
and his friends contribute even in an indirect way
to this deserving object. The Government, after-
first taking the whole matter out of the hands of
the police, were in a quandary as to what action
should be instituted, Baker, in order to embarrass
them, throwing all the responsibility on their
shoulders. The Premier was away in Melbourne,
and the whole decision of the matter rested with
Homburg, Attorney-General and acting Premier,
who was a colleague of Kingston’s in the last
Playford Government, and who keenly felt the
awkwardness of his position, more especially as
he knew that Baker was eagerly waiting to take
advantage of any slip he might make. Peters-
wald, Commissioner of Police, is a member of thu
same club as Mr. Baker.

Sydney Garrick is looking a little less
empty and desolate this week, and “ Pink
Dominoes” is on the bill. At the same time it
seems hard to believe that all the world used to
howl with mirth over this same comedy. “Pink
Dominoes ” was done to death and ashes years
ago, and when its rouged and plastered face looks
out from the programme it suggests the paralysed
grandmother of the ballet doing a last hop on the
road to the sepulchre. No old play-goer can
laugh heartily at the piece now unless some one
stands over him with a club to make him do it.
The strength of the Garrick Co. consists, as
before, of Messrs. Diver, Sterling Whyte and
Cannam —particularly of the two former. Misses
Alice May and Flora Hastings are also extenuat-
ing circumstances, but the rest of the party suffers
from some petrifying complaint which is probably
incurable. The case isn’t one for medical science,
but in The Bulletin’s opinion the
show ought to be diagnosed by a
timber-merchant.
*■ * * *
Meston’s freshly-caught canni-
bals are on view at Bondi Aquarium,
and a fearsome crowd they are.
They are mostly of great size, and
a few are of alarming ugliness, but,
taken as a whole, they are more
beautiful than might have been
expected. The Australian who has
gone out into the wilds and success-
fully stood off the circumjacent
heathen in his lair, where he is
present in great quantities and
anxious for slaughter, has generally
returned to describe him as a bow-
legged horror with no neck and a
gnarled and crooked back, but
Meston’s specimens are all well-
shaped and reasonably graceful.
They are alleged to include samples
of the Oirakillas, Kitoondas,
Coongarries, Wookas, Injalahjos,
Eeas, Owanzillas, and other un-
speakable tribes, and some particu-
larly stern and beetle-browed ruffians
come from the spinifex desert, and
have the dust of the wilderness
still in their hair. They are all
partially clothed and vociferous,
and the atmosphere at Bondi is
full of spears, boomerangs, nulla-
nullas, war-dances, devil-dances,
mask-dances and frantic corrob-
boree. A long letter from a
well-meaning correspondent about
the spiritual prospects of the
aborigines is crowded out.
«* m *
Sydney Royal was announced
to re-open last Monday with
“Falka,” Miss Clara Merivale
being manageress and star performer. On the
previous Friday, however, George Rignold, who
claims to own the Australian rights of that not
too cheerful opera, obtained a legal injunction and
put his boot down on the show'. Consequently
the Royal hangs fire, and both parties are ex-
plaining violently in the papers, or their friends
are explaining for them. The lady’s story seems
to be that she is cr-r-rushed by the iron hoof of
oppression and that a bad and jealous man has
applied the torch to her prospects and burnt her
to the ground ; and the other side of the case sets
forth that Mr. Rignold is a long-suffering manager
who only asserted himself when he found a pre-
suming female going bald-headed for his copyright
and adding insult to premeditated injury. Mean-
while, “Falka” is in abeyance.
** * *
Premier Dibbs having withdrawn his pro-
hibition and declared the strike “off,” Miss
Jasia Scherazade’s lion performance is again one
of the attractions at Fillis’s Circus. It now tran-
spires that Sir G. Republican Dibbs had not seen
the exhibition when he issued his ukase; some
hysterical person had told him about it, and on
that second-hand evidence he straightway pro-
ceeded to close up the industry wlureby a good-
looking and deserving lady gained her livelihood.
As he now tacitly acknowledges that he made a
B articular fool of himself in so doing, The
ULLETIN wants to know if he has apologised to
the lady for his wildly-irresponsible conduct, or if
he sees his way to compensating anybody ? The
story of his cave-in is instructive. When the pro-
hibition was first issued, Miss Scherazade called
on Premier D. in ordinary walking dress, but
found him adamant. But later on Dibbs was
lured to the circus, where the lady is alleged to
have appealed to him in fancy costume, and when
she backed him into the corner between the laugh-
ing hyena and the baboon with the white whiskers,
and laid the matter before him in full, he fell back
heavily against the snake-cage and gave up right
there.
*# * *
Both the Sydney pantomimes are on the
high tide of prosperity, and the Shakespearean
comedy at the Criterion is doing better business
than that revered but ruinous dramatist has done
in Australia for many days. Rickards’ Minstrel
Go. and Wirth’s Circus at the York-street
skating-rink are also paying speculations at pre-
sent, and though it seems almost certain that,
among all these shows, at least one must burst at
an early date owing to the general insolvency of
the city, the question of where the burst will take
place seems as far from settlement as ever. Pro-
fessor John Macmahon has just completed a series
of six medical lectures at the Royal Standard on
“ The new Science of Healing without Drugs or
Operations,” and lias gone on tour in the northern
towns.
** * *
The Liliputian Opera Co. is running fie
pantomime of “ Aladdin ” at Adelaide Royal to
very good houses, but the performance would be
more comprehensible if its “localisms” did not
belong to places many hundreds of miles away
from Saintopolis. At Hudson’s Bijou, Emerson’s
Minstrels are doing fairly well with ancient Martin
Simonsen in front.

One week’s experience of “ The Forty
Thieves,” at Melbourne Princess’, confirms a long-
standing suspicion that the secret of pantomime
success lies in coin of the Realm. The manage-
ment buys its “ unprecedented triumph ” with
MISS HILDA SPONG.
(Late of Her Majesty’s Theatre Co.)
cash in advance, and reduces “standing room
only ”to a sordid question of quids. If William-
son and Musgrove order material enough for a
gorgeous annual their customers rush the premises
as a matter of course. An experienced stage-
manager dresses the shop, so to speak, and ulti-
mately the Firm comes along to see that the trade
in seasonable splendour is properly pushed. On
the present occasion the Princess’ is more than
well stocked, and all the goods will soon be dis-
played to advantage. Indeed, Mary Weir was
displayed to the best advantage in a Serpentine
Dance on the opening night, after which she dis-
appeared from the festival at one fell swoop.
Perhaps the vision of skin-tinted tights, whirling
midst fleecy clouds of gauze, was murdered by a
johnniefrom the reserved stalls, whom her outlines
had driven mad. Anyway, Mary was, at time of
writing, as mysteriously missing as Mat. Davies’s
mace. She responded to one tumultuous encore
and vanished. On second thoughts, we fancy that
the angels came and carried her away. Consider-
ing how few items were encored in the early
lights of this entertainment, tt was especially
cruel to purloin Miss Weir. Why didn’t some-
body burgle the everlasting Faust Family, or
remove a duet between Lonnen and Katie Barry
by stealth ? Such articles would not be missed.
# # #
The forty thieves, taken in a gang, are a
prime lot of darlings, adapted by Nature for
sneaking the tender feelings of any good Samari-
tan who might happen to fall amongst ’em.
The Captain, Miss Lelia Roze, is also a nice, quiet
sort of robber, with a lurking sense ot humour
that requires drawing out by Lieutenant Has-
sarac Snazolle. Snazelle himself has wanted
drawing out hitherto. He makes a promising
start by producing a bottle of whisky from his
camel’s hump, but afterwards he is left to wander
like a mitherless bairn in quest of “ bits of fat.”
They seem to have purchased Snazelle for a special
attraction, and put a wet blanket over him. The
rorty artist stands and sighs for chances to be
funny, and pours his melancholy into depressing
ballads. However, the first faults of a pantomime
areeasily corrected. Lonnen, as Ali Baba, activeand
energetic as ever, monopolises most of the inanity,
and Katie Barry is an indefatigable Ganem. Yet
Katie’s talent seldom soars above the indefatig-
able. Rexo and Reno, the contortionists, con-
tribute an act which is wonderful without being
revolting, and Alice Lethbridge simply walks
through the parts where she isn’t dancing. Miss
Lethbridge is an admirable skirt-dancer, but her
line of business is becoming exhausted. Now that
the public appetite for Serpentine has been
whetted, solid drapery, however artistically
managed, is de trap, and a pale pink Alice gleam-
ing through the lime-lighted cobwebs would be
much more acceptable—
E’en as the shine from well-worn pants
Is rubbed aw'ay by turpentine,
So fades the mazy-skirted dance
Before the fleshly Serpentine.
A number of other characters are as nothing com-
pared with Ali Baba’s donkey. In fact, none of
the principals are yet as diverting as the donkey,
who deserves special mention along with Gordon’s
scenery, the ballets, costumes from Hingland, and
other firsLclass fittings.
** * *
“ Babes in the Wood,” at the Royal is not
a wildly extravagant affair, as far as money is con-
cerned, but it contains a procession of kings and
queens, from William the Conqueror to V.R.,
which ought to hurry up the coming Republic
considerably. With this melancholy pageant
George Coppin bids fair to annihilate local
“loyalty.” An Anarchist club, sleeping on a
heavy banquet of underdone pork, couldn’t dream
such a slop-made lot of mediaeval monarchs as are
here seen staggering down the stage in couples.
King Stephen administers the death-blow to faith
in Norman blood, and Henry Vee is a distinct
libel on George Rignold. As for the lady on
whose empire the sun never sets, she can only be
regarded as an uncalled-for jest. It were
easy to procure a very stout, very short, and
very rubicund old lady to impersonate our
reigning Sovereign, and when Mr. Coppin orders
the National Anthem to play in honour of a com-
paratively young, tall, slim and pale super, of
amiable appearance, one feels that he must be
poking borak at the blessed Throne. Gourlay,
Geo. Carey, Harry Shine, Poulton and John
Forde appear frequently during the evening,
without awaking too much mirth; also, the dis-
ordered narrative includes a few performing dogs,
and a bevy of appalling infants, who knock
hiil eons din out of drums. Ada Bemister, the
new “ principal boy,” was handicapped in the
first instance by a bad cold, but the recovery of
her voice has made little appreciable difference to
the tout ensemble. Ada is a smart, commanding
kind of lady, who otherwise fails to realise
the expectations raised by Billee Barlow’s en-
gagement of twelve months back. The rival
pantomime is badly in want of a Billee for that
matter. Another serious disappointment is Miss
Bella B isliall’s alleged serpentine dance, which
doesn’t justify its title in either theory or practice,
or excite much enthusiasm. The limelight man is
a weak feature in this item, but, if all the original
effects were available, the fair dancer’s inability
to whirl fast enough would still be felt. Donato,
the one-legged Spaniard who made a spiral stair-
case of iiis wrap in days agone, could have given
many points to the new serpentine generation.
Artist Brunton comes out strong in a clever,
gaudy, transformation scene, and a short harle-
quinade terminates with the tragic death of the
clown (Shine). This is just as it should be. An
infuriated masher, weary of suffering the usual
indignities, fires a pistol at his chief tormentor,
and the painted miscreant rolls dead with a bullet
in his heart as the curtain descends. The corpsed
clown may be a staggerer for the children, but
adults regard him as a step in the right direction.
Next year we may expect the harlequinade to
begin with a similar ghastly murder, and e»4
with the clown’s funeral. /
** « *
The musical and firewood festival at the
Exhibition filled a Christmas want, although
there was soon a noticeable waning of public
interest in the chop-and-saw competitions. A
log-chopper on active service is no doubt a moving
spectacle, but life was a deal too short for the
waits between the axe. The present writer hung
around the festival for 30 minutes without seeing
anything save lumps of timber and a few sup-
posed heroes in the act of measuring them, so he
expressed a hope that the best chopper might
ultimately win, and walked sadly away, thinking
of George Washington as he went.
** * *
At the Opera House and Alexandra there
are always fine open spaces for the people who
incline towards long suffering heroines, and love
to hoot at the beast that “ shook ” the missing
documents in the prologue. Gloomy dramas of
domestic interest are appealing to the outside
world at both these theatres, but the world rejects
their invitation with a cold, hard stare and whoops
for frivolity. The music halls and many other
places that are open to be explored for a shilling,
or less, seem to drive a good trade, and a really
first-class entertainment at twopence, including
refreshments, would do better still.

Bill Holloway’s performance in “ Lear,” now
advertised in the Australian press, are simply re-
minders .that Irving has bought the souls of many

champagne.

Old friend Emily Fitzroy, sitting in the dress-circle of a
London theatre, is said to have been mistaken by several
people for Mrs. Langtry. The fair Fitzroy was certainly
one of the most fetching actresses who ever showed her
teeth on the Australian stage.
Lnscomhe Searelle, who hasn't grown a hit except in
girth, is in London once more, seeking for talent, writes
T»e Bulletin’s correspondent. The tour of Miss Gene-
vieve Ward and W. H. Vernon in South Africa, under his
auspices, was most successful from every point of view.
So much so, that the engagement was extended to double
the time originally contracted for. Vernon speaks highly
of the appreciative audiences they played to. They played
Shakespeare for 42 nights to £!»00 a w eek. Searelle, the
theatrical boss of South Africa, appears to he a wealthy
man now. He says that what we hear about Boer in-
tolerance is all nonsense. The population is English, and
wants to keep the Transvaal a Republic, and the only
means of doing so is to he nominally governed by the
Boers. The best feeling exists between (he two races
now, except In a few cases amongst the old hands.

Dear Bulletin. Recently,'on several occasions, I at-
tended the rehearsal of a pantomime which is now running
in a certain theatre in an Australian city. The greater
number of the people engaged for the production are
young girls and children some of the latter mere mites —
and to these the stage manager, almost every minute,
made use of the most horrible and disgusting expressions,
This hog’s language was bad enough, but his treatment of
the unhappy youngsters was even worse. He was con-
tinually dragging the poor little wretches about in a
brutal fashion, and on one occasion I saw him, when a
small boy made a false step in a march, knock the young-
ster down and thump him. The lessee of the theatre who
employs this beast has a very good name for gentleness
towards his employes, and it is only right to say that he
was not present on any of the occasions to which I have
referred—in fact, he was absent in another province at the
time. What is stated above is in no w r ay exaggerated,
it is, on the contrary, rather a mild account of the lowest
exhibition of blackguardism I have ever witnessed. —H.B.
Titheradge had occasion to call at the Age office, the
other week, with a view to asking a favour from David
Syme. The great man is not an inveterate theatre-goer,
and he found a difficulty in remembering where and when
he had heard the name of Titheradge. “lam an actor,”
said the comedian, and Syme replied, “Ah-h, ye-ess ” in
a way that seemed to suggest that he had rather thought
his distinguished visitor would turn out to be champion
wrestler or the captin’ of the South Melbourne football-
team. Titheradge may have felt disgusted at the news-
paper proprietor’s indifference to stage celebrity, but the
tonic he received in David’s private office ought to do
him a world of good. Tith., between ourselves, is one of
those highly superior actors who look surprised and in-
terested every time they hoar that there’s a paper called
The Bulletin with pictures in it. “But isn’t that the
same paper as the Argus ? ” he will usually say.
Royce made his re-appearance in London at a Drury
Lane matinee for the Theatrical Fund benefit. The
generation that has “ come on” since Teddy' sailed for
Australia could hardly believe that he was reckoned a
goner several years ago when paralysis struck him down.

Joe Jefferson intends to play “Rip Van Winkle” for
another year before retiring from the stage. Sad news.
Jefferson’s Rip became, long since, one of those wonder-
fully elaborate performances which are a burden to the
flesh of the audience, and as a dramatic fetish he is very
little more satisfactory than dear old Johnny Toole.
Even the Divine Sarah couldn’t obtain permission to
produce “ Fedora ” in St. Petersburg, recently. A play
which breathes a casual suspicion of n Nihilistic murder
is held to be dangerous.

Victorian Orchestra men, who thought very little of
Clarke as a conductor, were generally ofjopinion that he
had a pretty taste in composition.

At a recent actors’meeting in London, presided over
by Henry Irving, it was decided that six performances
were to constitute a week’s work and that- rehearsals
during any season of less than four weeks should be paid
for at the Vale of half a day.

production. The heroine carries on the Liverpool business

Melbourne, although probably the author never heard of
them.
Harry Leston, the well-known comedian, has given up
what promised to be a brilliant stage career and taken to
teaching elocution. There is a great deal of sea-travelling
in the life of an Australian actor, and Leston can’t even
go down to Manly without suffering such horrors that he

with his smiling countenance and his carpet-hag would he
calculated to arouse the Mahommedan population to

Besides, he would probably be hung as a spy before he got
through Afghanistan. His explanation that he was a

When Sara Bernhardt first visited America she was
interviewed by a hounceahle female reporter. Sara didn’t
speak English in those days, and the she-reporter didn’t
speak French, so an interpreter had to he provided. It
was a bad time for ih > interpreter. The literary female
kept asking questions which he dared not translate lest
Sara saould tear his hair, or set. her menagerie on him,
and the Bernhardt took a dislike tT the invading harridan
from the very beginning, and insisted I hat she slim 11
“get out.” The dialogue was something like this.
R' porter: “Ask her if it is true that she took a death
mask of her husband s face, and afterwards exhibited it
at the Salon?” Interpreter: “She asks, madam, if you
do not find these east winds very trying.” Sara: “Tell
the creature that it is her bonnet that I find tryin-'-
maddening—impossible. Tell her if she floes not leave
the room I shall have to or-r-rush her boniet with a sofa-
cushion.” They both got away alive, though it was a
close call. But to this day the interpreter falls into a
cold perspiration when he thinks of what Sara would
have done to him if he had translated that sho-roportcr
verbatim.
