E.F.
The Brisbane Worker’s special correspon-
dent, just returned from a trip in a South Sea
slaver, tells how the Kanakas are “ recruited ” :
You may tell a bread-and-butter miss in a Brisbane
church that the Kanaka comes of his own accord. The
man who has been there knows better. They are simply
bought and sold - sold into slavery. Nobody on board a
labour schooner pretends that it is otherwise. How could
they ? The thing- is an obvious, matter of tact transaction
between the Queensland Government and the relatives of
the kidnapped youth. See what happens. The ship
drops anchor ; the recruiting agent pushes off in his boat
with a bundle of “trade” tomahawks, knives, pipes,
tobacco, beads and so forth, tied up in a bright-coloured
scrap of calico; darkies begin to crowd the beach ; the
officer holds the bundle up to their admiring gaze ; every-
body knows that it represents the price of a boy ;
presently out step a couple of old men with a youngster
marching between them ; the first sacrifice is made ; the
boy tumbles into the boat and his elderly friends march
off in triumph with the gewgaws. Perhaps when he gets
on board he will fail to make himself understood.
We took one boy back to Queensland with us who was
absolutely dumb the whole of the voyage. No one could
speak his language. A batch of seven others spoke in a
tongue that no one on board understood. Any appeal
under such circumstances would have been utterly use-
less. One after another the boys come down to the beach
in charge of their “friends,” the bundles of trade are
handed over, and the silent bargains are struck. If the
boy now dead for a while eats none of the funeral meats
he is equally out of it when the time comes for his return
to life. The boys we landed were always plundered. If
they were bushmen the salt-water tribes who held the
beaches pounced down upon them before their own
people had a chance of making good their claim ; if they
happened to belong to the tribe in possession of the beach
then they had the privilege of being robbed by their
friends. In either case the result of their three years’
thrift was appropriated, and the very clothes they wore
scattered broadcast among the community. That, no
doubt, is the secret of the traffic. Just as in “ civilised”
countries, out of regard for their own comfort the rich
send the poor into hades, so in the South Sea Islands the
strong and lazy ship the •young and active across to
Queensland.
The Greatest of Australia’s
Dead.
There stands on the shelves of the cataloguing
room of Melbourne Public Library a range o
well-bound folio volumes entitled “The Parlia
mentary History of Victoria.” They represen
an idea of Sir Redmond Barry’s. Take down i
volume to the right hand and open it, and yoi
will find it a compilation of blank pages. Taki
down a volume to the left, and you shall discen
the profile, as it were, of the distinguished jurist’]
plan. A short, squat page of Hansard confront:
you, inserted between two magnificent folit
sheets. “On those sheets,” said Sir Redmond
“I would have mounted a commentary on th<
debates. It should be composed of extracts fron
the public press, documents of State, the letters
of public men, their portraits, their tailors’ anc
their washing bills even, and such like flotsam ol
the hour. A hundred years hence some historian,
because we have done this, shall of his doits pay
for a perpetual mass for our souls’ salvation ! ’
Then, pausing, he drew a finger-tip across the
bands of half-a-dozen volumes, and, proceeding
(a suggestive note of reverie in his tones), said :
“Particularly should everything bearing upon
Mr. Higinbotharn’s career be collected. The his-
torian will want to know what sort of a man he
was that challenged the Constitution and—and
the Empire. Those six volumes should be Mr.
Higinbotham’s! ” " With the touch of epigram
with which he frequently adorned his obiter dicta
the Judge added : “And the historian would find
the bills paid !”
# # #
That range of great volumes stands to-day but
the simulacrum of a history. The petty hirelings
of the State have no sympathy with ingenuous
enthusiasm such as the Judge and Scholar was not
ashamed* to own, and they have left the book as it
was when Sir Redmond deposited it in their
charge, ft is the veriest shell. But what would
Australia not give at this moment for “ those six
volumes” that the framer of the plan, the creator
of the noble Library which by that plan he would
have endowed with a priceless treasure, had thus
conceived? To gain them, replete with the sym-
bols of the time; eloquent of its passions, its
enmities, and its aspirations ; and significant—in
their reports, their caricatures, their satires, their
ana, their mots, their imprecations—of the agony
of national travail, Australia would sacrifice every
other page of locally-recorded speech she possesses.
For those six volumes would have told the mighty
dramatic story of the birth epoch of Australian
democracy, and would have shown as nothing
now extant can show the virile proportions of the
protagonist of the drama—George Higinbotharn,
* « #
There was no objective point of democratic
sympathy and aspiration that was not, from 1863
to 1870, assailed in Victoria by the forces of privi-
lege and plutocracy. Manhood suffrage, the
popular power of the purse, absolute and not
merely nominal self-government, the right of an
Australian community to conserve industrial
interests without reference to unstable economic
theory or to the mercantili.-m of a foreign state,
the abolition of electoral and representative privi-
lege, and national education, were all unsettled
questions then. Through the storm and stress of
the period they passed from the region of doubt
into the category of solved problems, and though
symptoms of reaction may have since been occa-
sionally manifested with respect to some of these
vital democratic issues, the set of the cur-
rents of national existence was, within those
years, finally determined, and the highest
tidemark defined. The battle of Constitu-
tionalism against an oligarchic officialdom had
been fought in New South Wales ; the conflict of
which it was the precursor and which was still
more tremendous in its intensity and in its scope
because it attached an indelible character to the
democratic institutions of Australia, was naturally
fought in the younger province. Wentworth was
the animating soul of the one; Higinbotham, a
greater Wentworth—the phrase is measured and
advised—that of the other.
• • #
We_ do not depreciate Wentworth when we
magnify the man who, with a stern simplicity
that fills the imagination with a grander solemnity
than the pomp and circumstance of state obsequies,
was last Monday returned to his mother earth.
the First of First-born Australians ” did
a great work when, for the welfare of
unborn generations it had to be done,
at the risk of the doer’s loss and at the
cost of obloquy. He had intellectual force and
the pervading fire of genius that constitutes the
orator, the statesman, and the patriot. But a
perfect magnanimity of soul he had not, for he
did not scorn to take advantage for his own benefit
of the degraded temper of his day, and he could
not rise above his own ambitions. The monstrous
Maori land land-grab, and his refusal to trust the
people absolutely because the people at last had
come to mistrust him somewhat, are stains upon
Wentworth’s greatness. Had N.S.W. possessed
a powerful and brilliant press, and the community
been one of freemen, it is conceivable that after
the Maoriland fiasco Wentworth’s influence had
been utterly demolished, and that to other men
would have fallen the glorious task of winning our
constitutional freedom. A sound press would
have declared that he had bartered his chance
of immortality for an ephemeral greed. Turn
upon Higinbotham the flashlight of similar
criticism, and the unchallengeable purity of his
public and his private life is your answer.
* » *
No biographer shall have to make apology for
Higinbotham. He took nothing from the public
to which be had not an absolute right; he took
less than he had a right to ; he took immeasurably
less than he gave. The intellectual force and the
pervading fire of genius were his, too ; but to them
he added an undimmed honour, a humility of
personal aim, a singleness of eye, a patience with
the people even when the people’s madness stung
him, and a dauntless sincerity and strength.
Wentworth, when he chose to be unscrupulous,
was supported by the abler section of an insipid
press, and had to contend only with its weaker
element, while outside the press his adversary was
an inert mass of officialdom entrenched behind
the traditions descended from a remote age.
Higinbotham, in his contest with Conser-
vatism, found it as aggressive as it was
able ; found, moreover, that it had en-
hated as allies the most brilliant band of
journalists and publicists ever controlled by a
daily journal in these provinces. It was a current
saying that, while of the M'Culloch-Higinbotham
Ministry but Higinbotham and Michie could
a a 'k' l *et as intellectually strong as the
Ministry far and away the ablest that ever took
office in Australia—could have been formed from
the Argus staff. These men of the pen gained
for the local contest the invaluable aid of the
British periodical and newspaper press. A letter
or two, and a solitary article apologetically in-
serted, made up the total published contributions
on the Liberal side, though the Conservative views
inundated every important magazine and journal.
One M estminster Review article, a superb piece
?< f 7 ri * ingr ’ denounced Higinbotham for
felonious and criminal” conduct, was the
joint work of two Argus men, and a man
who afterwards became a puisne Judge;
the reply to it was refused insertion. The
octopus of British mercantilism, fearful of
the threatened loss of most profitable markets,
threw out its tentacles and sucked almost
to the point of exhaustion the financial and com-
mercial energies of Melbourne; on the other hand,
the potential force embodied in the .sentimental
attachment for England of the Britons who
formed the bulk of the Victorian population, was
stimulated to an unreasoning excitement by their
‘ home ” correspondents. Men to whom England
had granted only penury and the croud privilege
of being someone’s serf, but on whom Victoria
had conferred manhood and independence, were
stirred on a sudden to war for their ancient chains
and to*peroeive glories in their former yoke. Down-
mg-street, and even the Throne, were included in
tlie forces of the Conservatives, The former
interfered with internal administration toa degree
it has never dared to do since, and the
visit of the Duke of Edinburgh was, beyond
doubt, but a card played from the Con-
servative hand. Against this tremendous
array of the Reactionaries, McCulloch led but half-
heartedly. Higinbotham, the lieutenant, was
really the general. With unfailing resource and
tact, and with never-flagging vigour and dan he
marshalled at once his company in Parliament and
his cohorts of tlm people. At once he was
The Porum’s champion ami the People’s chief.
* # #
He met ins adversaries at all points, whether
they sheltered themselves behind the practice of
the Constitution or behind considerations of expe-
diency. There has been no leader like him in
Australian politics none with the dash and
daring, the courage and rectitude, the culture and
resource. He broke the forces of Uowning-street,
and he took the heart out of the most arrogant and
exclusive Chamber Australia has ever known. He
won from privilege and class ground they have
never since been able to recover—ground they
never shall recover while Higinbotham’s name
retains its magic of conjuration.
* # #
That was Higinbotham s political achievement,
and for it he had his reward in the fervent trust
and affection of the people, and the enmity of a
petty local cabal which superseded him by the un-
speakable Bent. When he re-entered Parliament
it was seen he was not the same man. It was not
AT il r. i iac * s * into Conservatism as
McCulloch, and Verdon, and Francis, and Service
did ; not so, for to the day he, died his heart swung
true to the poles of Democracy. In the storm and
exhilaration of the combat it was not understood
how the fiery spirit was burning up the fibre of
the physical clay. But when he reappeared in the
House how he had suffered was made apparent.
Of the brightness and strength of his intellect
there was no diminution, nor were the passion and
fire, the grace and point, of his oratory modified.
But he spoke seldom, and, as a constructive
statesman, manifested himself still less frequently.
He sought to carry proposals for land-reform,
which would have made Victoria the most ad-
vanced and contented country in the world, but he
was defeated, chiefly by the aid of men who are
novv waiting the paralysing touch of the law upon
their shonldersforparticipationin land-boom frauds
that would have been rendered impossible by his
scheme of legislation. And he advocated “as of
far-reaching moral value” the abolition of the
distinction between law and equity. These were
almost his only notable attempts to democratise
Victorian institutions in his later parliamen-
tary life, and they were impotent of practical
result. Nevertheless, they proved of inestimable
worth, for they showed the soul of the man was
inalienably the people’s. He was no “lost leader,"
though, by physical weakness, already foredoomed
to the splendid exile of the Heneh.
As a J «dge. he was of classic proportions. To
most great lawyers, the elevation to judicial rank
l8 > 80 as the public are concerned, a foretaste
of oblivion. But by two special acts—one of sua-
tajned endeavour through long years, and the
2j( ie . r spontaneous and momentary George

Three pearl-shelling divers have died
within tvyo months at Samarai, New Guinea.
Over-confidence caused them to stay too long
under very deep water, the pressure rupturing, it
is supposed, some of the internal organs. Two of
the men were quite paralysed when drawn up to
the surface. Each had tallied exactly 32 shells.

bourne ex-policemnn. who once registered a
•solemn vow to shoot Matthias Larkin, has died.
Laddn s frauds reduced his former admirer,
Heffernan, to poverty and lunacy— in fact, the
p(K)r devil became so mad that he ultimately shot
himself instead of the other party. This is about
the tenth suicide directly attributable to the ras-
canty of “ respectable ” criminals lightly sen*
tenced, or not likely to be sentenced at all—on
earth.

Jimmy Munro’s official explanation of his
return to Victoria is sweet and simple. He has
come “to ascertain what charges, if any, are pre-
ferred against him in a matter in which lie is
entirely ignorant.”—
“IF ANY.”
Where ignorance is bliss
’Tis folly to be wise.
To things that went amiss
Some people shut their eyes ;
Jim don't know what occurred
To scandalise so many,
Nor the charges they preferred,
If any, if any—
Nor the charges they preferred,
If any.
To him it's all as Greek ;
lie really can’t explain
\\ hy certain people Shriek
And agitate in vain.
Although the fools averred
They’re left without a penny,
What charge have they preferred,
If any, if any ?
What charge have they preferred.
If any ?
O happy innocence!
O fair, unruffled brow 1
Jim cannot see the sense
Of kicking up a row.
He’s blithesome as a bird,
And gay as good Sir Benny ;
What charge can he preferred,
If any, if any ?
What charge can be preferred,
If any. ,

An Age correspondent mentions that Mrs.
Jeannie Lockett, a lady who had acquired some
reputation in Sydney literary circles, has written
a posthumous novel, which has just been pub-
lished. Poor Mrs. Lockett must be included in
the list of immortal writers.
Pious Wesleyans are beginning to look
askance at Editor Fitchett, who still draws the
bulk of his income from the denomination. They
do not approve of the practical denial which he
has given of the Scriptural decision that “Ye
cannot serve God and Mammon.”
Gahan, the champion Melbourne vagrant,
is dead. In the past he was a crack sporting
attorney. In the District Court lie used to bo a
smart cross-examiner, in a natty, slap-up tweed
suit from a fashionable Block tailor. No one ever
saw such a change on any stage.
The suggestion that Mrs. Parnell’s bank-
ruptcy was connected with the “ looking-up ” of
the Land League funds turns out to he another
Tory invention. The lady has simply got in
front of her private means, the limits of which
have been much overstated, in just an ordinary
womanly way.

E. P. Nesbit, next to Symon, Q.C., is
considered the best lawyer in Adelaide. He is
over six feet, is built like a telegraph-pole, and
carries almost as much electricity. Poetry and
the ladies are the solace of his restful hours, and
he did much to boom Mrs. Brown-Potter and
Miss Edith Bland while they were in Adelaide.
Parkes’ new house at Annandale (Sydney),
according to a correspondent, was vacant for two
years before he took it by reason of an alleged
ghost—the spectre of a former occupant. It isn’t
haunted any longer, though. The spectre took one
terrified look at the new tenant, and then gathered
up its spectral carpet-bag and fled from the scene.

Neither of the Sydney morning papers, in
sketching the great George Higinbotham’s career,
made the least allusion to his consolidation of the
Victorian statutes the greatest intellectual under-
taking yet carried out in the Australias. In this
work, he was, it is true, assisted by some young
barristers, but the conception, plan and method,
and detailed revision were entirely his own.

In his book, remarks a correspondent,
Parkes traces his career in Australia, commencing
with his employment as a farm-labourer, hue never
a word does lie say of the bad tops be used to make
in Sydney, and which brought down upon Ins
head the anathemas of every boy whose “ Parkes’
top ” was split by a “Tom Morriser.” Tom was
noted for the superiority of his manufacture. He
had a shop in Campbell street, where all Cape’s
and the Normal Institution boys used to buy their
“ spinners.”
The Bishop of North Queensland, lately
travelling by coach from the coast to a goldfield,
stayed for the night at a well-known bush hostelry
kept by a co-religionist of bis, who, never having
had the honour of receiving a bishop under her
roof-tree before, was untiring in her efforts to
please. She hovered round his lordship’s chair
during his meal, lifted flies out of his tea with her
finger, presented him with a raw onion from her
pocket as a delicacy, and paid him many other
charming little attentions.
The last list of new Js.P. up South con-
tained the name of an experienced, long-headed
citizen of under 30 years of age, who, three weeks
after his marriage with a certain politician’s
daughter, deserted her. He went on a wild,
drunken orgy to Maoriland, where he exhausted
both his funds and his vitality. He was forwarded
home from the hospital in which he ended his
holiday, upon a guarantee from his friends (they
were afraid to trust him with money) that his
fare would be paid on his arrival in Sydney.
The Argus professes to scoff at the “singu-
lar suggestion that Sir Henry Parkes would be
raised to the peerage but for the unfortunate fact
that he is not ‘overwhelmingly rich.' ” It happens
that the reference to Sir 'Enery's impecuniosity
as a disqualification for a coronet is the one feature
of the suggestion which is not singular. Compared
with an ordinary brainless, apotheosised brewer,
the old man from Brummagem would make a most
estimable peer, and the fact that he doesn’t run a
brewery is the only earthly reason why he ought
not to bo created a marquis to-morrow.

The Victorian public debt on 30tb June
was £16,711.287.
Speaker Jenkin Coles, of S.A. Assembly,
expects knighthood before next Parliament meets,
Le Caron, it is said, starts a weekly politi-
cal journal in London. To be called the Spy, no
doubt.
Another member of the N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Council is in financial difficulties, and his
schedule is expected to be filed any day.
“The business of a Radical representative
n Parliament is to keep the Ministry up to the
Radical high-water mark ” (Labouchere).
The London Tory journal, Imperial
Federation, alluding to Dibbs’ enhandlement,
contemptuously speaks of him as “ the Dibbs of
other days.”
According to the Brisbane (Q.) Courier,
MTlwraith has left his resignation “in the hands
of a trusty friend, for use in the event of any un-
seen emergency.”
Thirteen is a proverbially unlucky num-
ber. The only law of first importance passed by
the thirteenth Parliament of S.A. was the enact-
ment of free education. .
Melbourne papers pathetically reiterate
hints of Mr. Shiels’ weariness of office and growing
disgust of place and pay. Shiels has bitten off
more than he can chew.
English papers say that an independent
Labour Party will never be formed in the British
Parliament, because each of its units fancies him-
self a “ heaven-born leader.”
Dr. Pearson has been appointed secretary
to the Victorian Agent-General, because Premier
Shiels pledged his political honour that the Pro-
fessor would not be so appointed.
The Australasian monopolistic press should
desist from running down Jay Gould. He was
only the true, logical expression of the grab-all,
individualistic system they so much admire.
MTlwraith to Hodgkinson : “ Can’t you
read this notice? ‘Croydon to Granite Creek
Land-grab Swindlecate : Trespassers on private
preserves will be evicted from the Cabinet. ’ Go !
Get !! ”
When Premier Downer (S.A.) announced
that he intended to “send ’ome ” for a new com-
mander somebody very pertinently asked if this
wasn’t Downer’s home? The question wasn’t
answered.
The slave-dhow Empreza has returned to
Queensland with 119 “ recruited ” black labourers,
of whom two only are Marys (females). The
proportion between the sexes is thus beautifully
preserved.
S.A. National Ass has nothing to say about
a “vote for Thrift.” Such a cry would be
dangerous in a province where even Conservatives
approve of One-man-one-vote, because they can’t
abolish it.
Griffith to Nelson : “ Scratch poor cocky
with an extra £lOOO per annum as Chief Justice.”
Nelson to Griffith : “ Scratch poor cocky with the
Chief Secretaryship.” A round game of “scratch
poor cocky.”
Shaw Maxwell appears to be jealous of
John Burns, and says that “ his egregious vanity
is the despair of all associated with him.” Fana-
ticism or vanity is a necessity for those who
pioneer reforms.
American monopolists are uniting to down
manhood suffrage. The mere attempt will awaken
the dormant American workers to a knowledge of
unused political rights and the neglected duties
dependent on them.
“How are we regarded at Home?’’ the
Standard asked Munro when he reached Mel-
bourne. “Asa band of thieves ! ” replied Jimmy.
This is very sad, but we cannot complain if we
are judged by certain of ourown “representative”
men.
The Protectionists should see that the
Age dialogue, “Freetrade Fallacies and Protec-
tion Proofs ” is reprinted and distributed in scores
of thousands throughout Australasia. It is the
most effective thing of the sort we have seen for
years.
E. G. Brown, who was absent from the
division on the late Dibbs censure motion, is a
brother of H. H. Brown who, though a nominal
Freetrader, voted for the Protectionist Govern-
ment. Those who know the Brothers Brown are
busy making deductions.
Premier-Knight Downer means to bring
an English officer to rule the S.A. militia,
‘because a local appointment would breed
jealousy and dissatisfaction.” Similarly, England
ought to take a “colonial” cut-throat to fill the
post of the Shook of Gampridge.
The Alpha of Dr. Pearson’s political career
was a jobbery douceur of £lOOO for a report (ever
since carefully pigeon-holed) on the Victorian
State-school system; the Omega will probably be
ms appointment to the Victorian Agency-General,
of which he is already the secretary.
Melbourne Argus says that the Queens-
land planter ‘.‘must be content to treat the
Kanaka as a free-labourer.” This is obviously a
Misprint. What the Argus meant to say was
that the Queensland planter is quite content to
treat the free-labourer as a Kanaka.
Nelson will probably bo the next Premier
of Queensland, it being freely stated that Griffith
will positively resign in February, and will advise
Norman to send for Nelson. How long the
Manila man will be chief oook of Kanakaland de-
Ponds on the result of the next elections.
Women canvassed hard against the two
arch-opponents of women-franchise in the Maori-
land Parliament—Fish and Fisher—when they
ran for the Mayoralties of Dunedin and Welling-
ton respectively. The pair had often won before
but they were beaten on the women’s votes.
When we consider that during the Balfou 1 ’
regime, so-called dynamite outrages didn’t ocean
though legitimate Irish agitation is ever most
active in periods of coercion, the presumption is
pretty strong that the late attempt on Dublin
Castle had its origin in a conspiracy of land-
lordism.
The Melbourne metropolitan district,
remarks the Age, returns 32 members to the Vic-
torian Legislative Assembly, yet spite of “ the
parting of the ways,” there is absolutely no Free-
trade party in the House. There are Freetraders,
but they preserve their seats by conducting them-
selves as Protectionists.
In an article on Australasian Federation,
which appeared in St. James’ Budget (London),
Hon. Edmund Barton is called the Victorian
Attorney-General, and is credited with having
brought forward a motion in “ the Victorian
House of Commons” in favour of Federation,
Let us federate —in the interests of geography.
When Cotton went home ill from the S. A-
Legislative Council one of the less virulent land-
grabbers agreed to pair with him. He was dead,
having passed away quietly in his sleep, when the
motion in favour of a Trades Hall Site came up
for settlement, but his pair was counted in respect
to the division. So he voted in death as in life.
The S.A. general elections take place in
April, and it is stated that Downer means to
ignore the Assembly resolution that all nomina-
tions should be simultaneous. The Council threw
out the bill limiting election-expenses to £lOO per
candidate and inflicting specially heavy penalties
for bribery. This legislation does not suit the
National Ass.
“ You Freetraders are not consistent for a
moment. At the very time the Freetrade mob
were shouting their hosannahs to President
Cleveland, the United States Congress was pass-
ing a law to restrict immigration. It would have
its goods imported free, but it would keep out the
people who made them. And this is a sign of a
Freetrade reaction ! —lhe Age.
Unmack is anxious to have a Queensland
Coalition Government to maintain law and order
against Anarchy. There is a lot of policy in
giving a dog a bad name. Why don’t the fight-
ing democrats of Australia stick the obnoxious
term where it fits? The “sehindycator,” slave-
traders, leper-breeders, and inflated grabbers, are
the only anarchists in the land.
There is so much dissatisfaction in Maori"
land with the imperfect character of last session’s
Shop Hours’ Act, that next session there will be a
demand even from employers that a day be fixed
for the weekly half-holiday, and that it be made
compulsory to close on that day. At present, a
man can close or not, as he likes, provided he lets
his assistants away on one afternoon a week.
It is alleged that a shindykit consisting
principally of Patterson, Harper, and O’Loghlen
—two howling bigots and an irresponsible gossoon
—will presently make a great effort to upset the
Shiels Cabinet and form a new Victorian Ministry.
O’Loghlen is to be Treasurer and borrow
£9,000,000 every year at 10 per cent., and Patter-
son and Harper are to suppress the trades-unions
and fire low.
Premier Shiels (Vic.) has one formula for
all awkward questions : “ It would not be in the
public interest to give the desired information.”
This short sentence has eaten into his system so
badly that if a member got up and asked him if it
was true that he had hidden a murdered infant up
a drain, he would probably arise, with a lack-
lustre expression in his eye, and grind out his old
remark quite unconsciously.
New South Wales Judges get £2BOO a year
and enormous travelling expenses, and when
they’re too old and silly to draw full salary they
retire on a pension of £lBOO, or so, yearly, though
their salary has been so high that they might
easily have lived on their savings. But a man,
disabled for life while working on the railway-
line, gets a lump sum of £25, and then is told that
he has no farther claim on the State.
Maoriland Govt, has, in exercise of its
statutory powers, just taken over the late “ Ready
Money Robinson’s ” Cheviot Hills (Canterbury)
Estate because the trustees would not agree to
pay land-tax on an official valuation of £304,826,
and insisted that it was only worth £260,220—at
which price Govt, snapped it up. There are
84,222 acres in the run, which is to be cut up.
This is the first case of the kind in Maoriland.
“ Whatever the planter might desire,”
remarks the Argus, loftily, “lie must be content
to treat the Polynesian as a free labourer.” Apart
from its blessed ingenuousness, this sentence is
valuable as affording a clear definition of the
“ free-labourer. ” The white “ free-labourer ” is
invited to extend his hand to the cheap nigger,
and call him brother. To what base uses do they
return who play into the hands of the monopolist!
Samuel Griffith arrived in Townsville in
the course of his N.Q. trip, just in time to find
his effigy hanging by the neck from a tree as
an expression of public enthusiasm. Two or
three big capitalists gave him a cordial welcome
and liquored him up at a hotel, but the bulk of
the population preserved an ominous silence. The
strangled image that danced at the end of the
rope expressed their sentiments more eloquently
than words.
In 1838, Harriet Martineau wrote to
Edward Smith Hall, of Sydney, that she would
visit Australia, “if health lasted.” From her
heart’s core” she craved “it might become an
ideal country.” If ihe Shades as they stroll in
the fields of Asphodel can curse, how noble
dreamers must imprecate the iniquitous brood of
politicians that have brought Australia to their
own slimy level ! This land was the idealists’
Utopia ! It is our politicians’ dung-heap.
That solid old Tory, G. W. Rusden, wails
bitterly in the dreary columns of Greater
Britain about the appointment of twelve demo-
crats to the Maoriland Upper House, and asks :
Would Englishmen understand the vilencss cf ihe wrong
done to New Zealand if they should see an attempt to
foist about 200 peers upon the House of Lords in order to
carry a Bill to set Ireland in flames?
Where are the “flames,” anyhow? Has Rusden
got them in his pocket, or what?
Of the 32 men really returned on the
labour ticket to N.S.W. Legislative Assembly 15
are Freetraders and 17 Protectionists. As for
nationality, 8 are Australians, of whom 5 are Pro-
tectionists ; 7 are Scotch, 5 of whom are Free-
traders ; 16 are English, of whom 12 are Protec-
tionists ; 3 are Irish, 2 of whom are Protectionists,
while the American and Maorilander who make
up the total are both Freetraders. The N.S.W.
Assembly (141 members) does not contain quite 50
natives.
When the Maoriland land-tax was imposed
last session, the croakers said it would never bring
in the required revenue. Tax - Commissioner
Crombie (an old Victorian pressman) said it
would, and estimated the receipts at ,£286,000.
That was six months ago. In the result, £284,000
came up to the days of grace, and another £2OOO
by post later. Good guessing, this. The Maori-
land income-tax was not payable until the end of
December, and that Crombie estimates at £92,000.
The Maoriland Ministry are jubilant.
“ Freedom of contract ” was once a phrase
that represented a human aspiration for liberty,
but that was in the days when feudality and serf-
dom made men and women an appendage of acres,
mere things that went with the land like the trees
and the stone quarries. Now, the conditions that
make this appeal for freedom having been merged
into environments only less odious, the shibboleth,
sanctified by the traditions and associations of by-
gone victories, is hypocritically used, ostensibly on
the side of liberty, but in reality as a cry to catch
the thoughtless and crush the wage-slaves “ rightly
struggling to be free.” The phrase is the same,
but the aspiration that animates it is very differ-
ent. It is the spirit of slavery masquerading in
garments cast off by resistance to tyranny and
oppression.
When Nelson becomes Kanakaland’s Pre-
mier, good dog Towzer and “Post and Rails”
Unmack will probably get “left.” Hodgkinson
will be nonest, and “Lands” Cowley will be dis-
pensed with. Both are Northern men, while
Towzer’s kennel is in the Wide Bay district.
Probable colleagues of Nelson are—MTlwraith,
Morehead, Barlow, Dickson, Morgan, and
Byrnes. A S.Q. team, and another mixing up of
autipathetics. Morehead and Dickson have in the
past been violent incompatibles, while windbag
Barlow enjoys the scorn of older parliamentarians.
The deadly feud between MTlwraith and More-
head—virulent, though of recent date —is to be
scummed over with the gold-beater’s skin of oof.
And fancy MTlwraith second-fiddle to Nelson,
whose parliamentary experience dates only from
within the last decade ! If the elections result in
the return of the new hotch-potch it will be lucky
if it lasts through one session.
Dear Bulletin, —Some instructive figures.
Salaries of the Supreme Court of N.S. Wales, any
of the judgments of which may, on appeal, be
reversed by a foreign tribunal, 16,000 miles distant
—Chief Justice, £3500 ; Associate-Judges, £2BOO.
Salaries of the Supreme Court of the United
States, admitted, even by unfriendly critics, to
be the highest and most impartial judicial body
in existence, and from whose decisions there is no
appeal—Chief Justice (an office filled in the past
by such historical figures as John Jay, Marshall,
Ellsworth and Chase), £2lOO ; Associate-Judges,
£2OOO. Salary of that automaton, the Governor
of the province of Victoria, £lO,OOO. Cleveland’s
pay when Governor of the great State of New
York, with a population of six-and-a-half millions,
£2OOO. Total pay of the forty-seven elected work-
ing governors of the states and territories com-
prising the Union, £34,260, considerably less than
the sum paid to the seven appointed nonentities
who nominally boss these “ kawlinies.”— Kanuck.
At last we are getting sanity in Free-
traders’ discussions of economic issues. Professor
Devas, of Ireland, one of the most lucid and wide-
minded economists of the day, thus writes :
It may reasonably be asked why, if the matter of Free-
trade and Protection is so simple, it has aroused such
lengthy and embittered criticism and controversy, not
merely among traders whose strong personal interests
will account for any mistakes in their reasonings, but also
among calm and impartial economists. The answer is
perhaps as follows. The classical economists were imper-
fectly aware of the principle of increasing returns, of the
principle of misdirected consumption, and of the anta-
gonism of national interests; they teere dazzled by the
example of England, the great cent re of wealth and trade,
the economic model of all eyes for some three quarters of
the nineteenth century, and a country precisely at that
time sin ndarly adapted for Freetrade ; and they tacitly
applied the condition of England to all other countries.
No wonder then, assuming what they assumed, and
ignoring what they ignored, that every Protectionist
appeared to them very like a fool or a knave. After
which it was difficult for Hie Protectionists to he very
calm in their answer or very clear. Remember besides
that, as the practical application of Freetrade or Protec-
tion turns on particular matters of fact, which are often
disputable, there is plenty of room for dispute.
Italics are The Bulletin's. It is about time
somebody emphasized the circumstance that a
Freetradt r can pen a common-sense statement.

THE CROWN CONSIDERING.
PREMIER SHIELS : “Any amount of consideration is expected from us in this matter.”
BERRY : “Quite right, William—keep on considering.”
What does Hhiels consider?
Does lie show his teeth and smile
At the narrative so neat
Of the merry Mercantile
And its final balance sheet —
Does he ask himself why was it
That a dividend they paid
From the moneys on deposit
When no profit had been made ?
Does the Premier consider
That Sir Matthew was a “kiddor”
Of the orphan and the widder
With whose capital he played ?
Now, what is Shiels thinking ?
Can the figures and the facts
He is slowly wading through
Satisfy him that the acts
Of the influential crew
Were superlatively honest,
And particularly square ;
Would he rather he were non cst
In this troublesome affair ?
Now, what is Shiels thinking ?
Is his heart within him sinking,
Or his other optic winking
At the one-time “ millionaire ? ”
What is Willie’s notion ?
Is the prima-facie case
Hot and strong enough for him ?
Does be wear upon his face
An expression stern and grim ?
Does he cry, “ The country bust is
(Though he don’t approve of slang),
But it shan’t be said that Justice
Has exploded with the gang ? ”
Does he tremble with emotion
Born of patriot’s devotion?
Can Willie raise a notion—
And does he care a hang ?

that my strength, and in an equal degree my spirits, were
waning f was losing all Interest in anything and in
everything. I nould not account for it. 1 at last began
to sutpect l might have the seeds of consumption sown in

Society
The pious wife of a Melbourne Judge sent
a Christmas card, with a few godly sentiments of
her own penned thereon, to each prisoner in the
Melbourne gaol. Thus a Judge sends a man to
prison for seven years’ hard, and then the Judge’s
wife calmly wishes him a merry Christmas and a
happy New Year. Talk about irony !

The applicants for almost every vacant
billet now advertised in Australia are told to
“state salary required,” and the work naturally
goes to the lowest bidder. This little game is
beginning to open the eyes of the obsequious, band-
boxy clerks who were not long ago so ready to
become special constables and “down ” themanlier
workers who stood out for a fair wage,
• * ' »
He was a new arrival, and knew as much
of the geography or size of Australia as newchum
Englishmen generally do. He had an uncle living
“ somewhere in Sydney,” and wished to find him.
Someone advised him to try the populous suburb
of Rookwood, where it was the fashion for most
of the residents to have their names engraved on
their residences. After wandering about there
half the day, and seeing nothing but tombstones,
the idea that he was the victim of a practical joke
began to painfully intrude itself into his thinking-
pan ; consequently, there is at least one newchum
in Sydney who has not a very exalted opinion of
the beastly colonials.
• » . •
A certain Australian evening paper now
charges double rates for the familiar baby-for-sale
advertisement. Recent revelations have convinced
the proprietary that baby-farming is practically
synonymous with murder, and its great soul has
revolted to such an extent that it won’t insert the
unclean thing for less than two shillings of blood-
money duly banged down on the counter.

One Williauls, while walking, last week,
in a Melbourne park, with a widow whom he was
about to marry, dropped dead. At the inquest it
was disclosed that he had deserted his wife and
committed bigamy with a girl whom in turn he
had deserted. He died of heart-disease. Too
many affairs of the heart play up with a man’s
aorta.

# • #
The old-time convict-regulations are still
tenaciously adhered to in Hobart. When a
drunken man is taken care of at the lock up (the
old prisoners’detention house) one of the questions
put to him regards his “ condition.” By this it
is not meant his bodily condition, but his status
in society—whether he be “B” (Bond), “F.C.”
(Free to the Colony) or “F.S.” (Free by Servi-
tude). Occasionally a man is unable to answer,
and he is duly logged as “F.C.,” and remains as
such for ever and ever. The scale of rates and
charges, imposed by the police and Bench, runs
something like this : Drunk, 55., or seven days ;
disturbing the peace, about ditto; gouging a
policeman’s eye out, 20s. or a month ; stealing a
bottle of lemonade, value 2d., three months; per-
forming an illegal operation, adjourned sine die ;
objurgating the present condition of affairs gene-
rally in a public place within the hearing of
persons, &0., six months hard labour.

In mostofacertaingroupuf Australian mining
townships each duly qualified medical man has a
cheap unqualified coadjutor who does all the rough
work. On one occasion tho skill and attention of an
assistant alienated the favour of the people from
his master who. piqued at his inferior s success,
promptly “sacked” him, and then industriously
advertised the fact that he was unqualified. Ihe
‘assistant suddenly left the district carrying with
him the good wishes of the whole community—
and the handsome wife of his former employer.

A young woman, the other day. jumped
off an express train between Haloombe and Ma-
king Maoriland, to rescue a bonnet which had
blown off. She saved her tile, and when they
stopped the train to bring along the corpse, coolly
walked aboard as though nothing out of the com-
mon had happened.

“You are a maniac!” said one lawyer to
another, daring the hearing of a case in the Box
Hill court, Melb., recently. “ You baboon . was
the reply. Then they went at it: “Sit down, yon
blackguard!” “You’re a common ruffian .
“Don’t touch me or I’ll knock you down.
“Will you, you bully!” “I’ll knock you into
mincemeat, you scoundrel!’’ etc., etc. The Bench
then chipped in feebly : “ Neither of the gentle-
men being in a fit state to go on with the case, it
is adjourned for a week.” But let a common
working person appear before that tench in us
labouring “get up,” or without a coat, and it will,
in a fit of righteous ferocity, gaol him tor
contempt.

Dear Bulletin, —I propose to start an
inebriate asylum on tho only true method, all
other methods being bogus and brass, and
originated by plausible impostors. It is entirely
my own discovery. I am arranging to obtain from
the Government a grant of 400 acres of rich and
heavily-timbered land on which to build a sana-
torium ; considering that the institution will be a
public benefit, there should be no difficulty on
this score. When the house is built—l have
arranged to get the money advanced on mortgage
—I will proceed to take in inebriates at the
extremely low rate of two guineas per week. The
system at first will consist chiefly of wood-
chopping. and I reckon that thirty inebriates will
be sufficient to clear and fence the land. After
that I propose to treat serious cases with plough-
ing, digging, planting potatoes, carting manure,
and similar remedies. lam firmly convinced that
vigorous exercise is the only cure for dipsomania.
My brother, who has a station on the Darling,
proposes to run it entirely with inebriates this
year, rates .£7O per year paid in advance. Any
patient who fails to observe the prescribed regi-
men—which will consist chiefly of shearing and
branding—may be promptly dismissed from the
sanatorium. —Yours, &c., L.

1 Currently reported that it was not con-

A case of curious interest to some prominent
Australians who, 15 years ago, entertained as
good “society” people, Mr. and Mrs. Morton
Tavares, was down for hearing early in October
at the Court of Queen's Bench, London. In those
days, “Mrs.” Tavares was an endurable actress
and a charming and very popular lady, while
“ Morty,” her tragedian-siiouse, was physically ft
dashing old boy, upright as Marshal Soult, with
dark eyes flashing with the glint of youthful fire.
Now, alas ! Morton Tavares appears in the pre-
cincts of the court a wreck ; feeble, blind, bent
double, his once black hair and moustache now
white, flawing and straggling—the living embodi-
ment of a “crushed tragedian to prosecute
Madame (now Mrs. Wise, the wife of the
captain of an ocean steamer) for alleged
slander, in that she declared she had married
Tavares, believing him to be a single man, where-
as he wickedly wasn’t, but already had a wife. It
was while “Mr. and Mrs. Morton Tavares” were
voyaging from Australia to the East in Captain
Wise’s ship that Mrs. T., otherwise Miss Surtees,
made the discovery that she was number two wife,
or whatever it may be called, whereupon she
promptly left the much-married tragedian—
Which when the cap’ll came to hear it he werry much
applauded what she'd d me, and immediately made her
his fust leftenant of the good ship, etcetera.
Now the grim old plaintiff declares that ho did
not marry Miss Surtees at all, and that she knew
it. “I may,” says he, “have been guilty of an
immoral act in living with her, but guilty of a
Chime NEVER-R-K. She lias accused me of
the terrible crime of bigamy, against which my
soul revolts. Therefore will 1 pursue her to the
gr-r-rave.” But lie didn't. At the last moment,
he withdrew the case on payment of his costa,
which Mr. Tom Rome cheerfully liquidated, to
avert annoyance to his wife who had been forced
reluctantly into the position of principal witness.
JIM’S WIFE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jim married a girl with a broken nose
Last Christmas Day but one,
And never his looks or his lips disclose
Regret for the deed he’s done.
His step falls light, his smile is bright,
He merrily cuts his joke ;
Yes, Jim’s as right as rain, in spite
Of the Missis’s nose that’s “ broke.”
For the real proof of a damaged “ tart ”
Lies in the eating, sure ;
And Jim finds joy in his Sarah’s heart,
And jam ”in her love so pure.
A handsome face about the place
Can’t banish a husband’s woes ;
But here’s a case of angel grace
Combined with a broken nose.
So, whensoe’er you may chance to meet
Upon the arm of Jim
A dowdy girl who don’t seem sweet,
Raise hats to her and him.
Some beauty may be here to-day,
To-morrow the blossom blows;
But fair for aye, and good alway,
Is the wife with a broken nose.
Melbourne.
E.F.
PLATO UP TO DATE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
She was married, but I loved her
As I had done all my life.
My love was simple, boyish love,
Which held her pare and kept me so.
Then we were thrown together much,
Danced, almost flirted, anil still I thought no guile,
But hugged my secret close, and worshipped,
Yes, worshipped her—’twas idolatry—and then,
You know the case, Blank versus Blank,
Dash co-respondent? That was she.
D.D.
ONLY A DREAMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sweet breeze over the waters danced
When the sun shone over the eastern hid,
But deep in a happy dream entranced
I sleep. lam only a dreamer still.
The sun has climbed to his noonday height,
And men are busy in mart and mill,
But I turn my face from the noonday light,
For I am only a dreamer still.
The sun is holding his westward way,
The sons of toil have toiled their fill,
But I have wasted the s immer day,
For I am only a dreamer still.
The evening bells from the convent ring,
The sky is red where the sun hrs set,
But I in my hammock idly swing,
For I am only a dreamer yet.
The leaves are stirred by acold wii d drear,
Ti.e glow Ins gone from the western hill,
The day is lost and the night is here,
And I am only a dreamer still.

an Australian vice-regal reception a few weeks

# * #
How is this for front? A certain civil
servant in a second-rate Australian city some time
ago called a meeting of all the other civil servants
m his town “ to consider a matter of the utmost
importance.” There was a large attendance. The
originator of the movement took the chair, and,
after giving way to a little brief emotion, he
arose, and, in a broken voice, addressed the
audience. The case, he said, was a very painful
one. there was a gentleman amongst them —a
gentleman known to them all, universally liked
and respected, perhaps the most popular man in
the room, and the bearer of a spotless reputation,
who had unfortunately given way to temptation.
He was short in his cash. It was impossible to
•mention the delinquent’s name, but he had
confessed all to him (the speaker) and implored his
assistance. (General emotion.) The only thing
to bs done was to take up a subscription, and the
orator solemnly laid £5 on the table as his own
contribution. Then the hat went round; every
chappie made a great effort, and £95 was realised.
The chairman took the money and thanked them
on behalf of the erring brother, and thev went
away, every man wondering whose cash was short,
and everybody suspecting his own particular boss
as the culprit probably it was because each man
suspected his own immediate boss that the con-
tributions were so abundant. That was two years
ago, and the secret just leaked out the other day.
The man whose cash was deficient was the eloquent
chairman himself. He was £3B short, and the
subscription, not counting his own £3, left him
£32 to the good.

All Jay Gould’s partners either died in
extreme poverty, went mad, suicided, or were
murdered. He cleaned out everybody. “ His
touch was death,” said “Uncle” Daniel Drew,
who was associated with him and Fisk in the first
great Erie swindles. Drew was cleaned out like
the rest, and lived for years in tatters and beer
and abject misery. He itad ideas at times of
grinding Gould’s skull on a doorstep, hut he never
had courage enough to carry them out. Nobody
ever had courage enough to bang Gould’s brain-
pan against a door-post, somehow.
A converted Jew named Cohen is now well
on his way towards the billet of Pope. This
individual was born of humble, old-clo’ parents,
and had no paiticular advantages, yet the other
day he was elected Prince Archbishop of Ohnutz,
the first and wealthiest episcopal see in Austria,
and one that has been occupied for over lOOOyears
by none but princes. The Archbishop of Ohnutz
is always created a Cardinal, according to an old-
established agreement between the Papacy and
the Austrian Empire, and after that there is just
one step between Cohen and the papal chair. He
will get there in time : the Cohens get everywhere
m time.
i hey worry much over Kingston, Q.C.’s,
letter challenging Baker to mortal combat. Some
say it “ exhibited signs of great mental excite-
ment, whilst others say it didn’t. The fact is
that nobody in Adelaide can read Kingston’s
handwriting without severe study— not even
Kingston himself. Next to Fysh, of Tasmania,
he ts the worst writer among Australasian public
men, as anybody can see if he examines the records
°f the last Federation Conference. His letters all
ex hi bit a sign of puppy-dogs with pens in their
paws scratching wildly on a white ground. Unless
he got this particular letter type-written, the
chances are that nobody not even Baker—
thoroughly understands it yet.

Dear Bulletin, —The imported principal
of a certain Australian teachers’ institution has,
when displeased, an absolutely original manner of
addressing those under her charge. She speaks
of them as “the grubs of the earth !” And this,
too, to their faces.—Tolo.
A new-chum, describing, in a letter to
England, the horrors of the Australian bush, stated
that they made pieces of rough tin serve as meat-
plates, using them three days one side and three the
other without washing, and boiled them clean on
Sundays, thereby getting a good supply of soup for
a change.
Cowley, Minister for Lands (Q.) had the
chilled steel cheek to announce, at Bundaberg, the
floating of another loan at an early date, though
the Q. Governinent has close upon a million and a
quarter of funds at deposit. The proposed new
loan is solely intended to furnish a fresh supply of
hard cash to a certain (manual institution suffer-
ing from excessive fix.-tiou of capital in un-
reahsable securities. The labour-party will fight
the new loan tooth and mil and will cable this in-
telligence to the principal Lonion papers if the
Cabinet attempts to float it.

bury College branch
of Maoriland Univer-
sity offered a prize
for an essay on “The
past and future of the
Maori race.” A
young Maori lad,
Apirana Tur up u
Ngata, sent in an
essay so thoughtful
and scholarly that it
easily took the prize ;
it has been published,
and has since formed
the theme -of innu-
merable article s.
Apirana is the young
son of a chief at
Waiapu, and is the
leader in a scheme
originating at the
Maori College of Te
Ante, famous on
Maoriland football
fields, for the educa-
tion of his race in
the laws of health,
morality, and sani-
tation. A strong
league has been
formed among Maori
students who speci-
ally equip themselves
with knowledge of
the subject and then
spend their vacations
iu tramping round the
pahs and instructing
tin- people. Apirana’s
University suucess
has inspired similar
ambitions among
other Maori students,
and many will now
go thro ug h the
course. Apirana
is going np for
his LL.B. degree
and seeks to become a barrister, but a younger
brother is learning physic, and will be the first
Maori duly qualified medico.
A Maorilaud fortune-teller advertises that
she “was born with a double veil; is the only
genuine seventh daughter of the seventh daughter
of the present age, tells the full name of your
future husband or wife, with age and date of
marriage; gives advice on divorce, contested wills
and speculations; removes freckles and family
troubles ; restores lost affections ; cures witchery,
fits, and all long-standing and mysterious diseases;
and tells your entire life, past and future, in a
dead trance; every hidden mystery revealed.”
And what worries us now is why this all-round
prophetess doesn’t tackle ingrowing toe-nails.

Reinachism.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Whether Baron Reinach, one of the
Parisian Panama Canal Co. directors, really died
of heart-disease, or apoplexy, or poison, or only
planted some stones in a gaudy sepulchre, I do
not propose to discuss. It is only worth remem-
bering that political crises and the delays in
forming fresh French Governments, permitted
enough time for a thoroughly scared live Reinach
—if alive, only—to find a corpse and decently bury
him in disguised clothing.
The time is, anyhow, evidently gone by when
men can successfully suicide on the lonely ocean
rocks, by merely leaving their clothes and pawn-
tickets behind them; and I take it that the
Reinach incident shows that the time has also
arrived when dumping stones in coffins will cease
to be a successful death-dodge. No, the world
having grown experienced, a corpse will hence-
forth always have to be provided.
Which reminds me of a most extraordinary
story that tells of what happened once in the Aus-
tralian Land of the Crocodile.
• # *
Bill Jumps was a prosperous trader, store-
keeper and sly grog-seller up at Ripbown, N.Q.
Bill made money so fast that it was a puzzle even
to his cutest and most intimate friends. At that
time the Commercial Banking Co., of Sydney, was
the fountain of nearly all N.Q. business transac-
tions. Money was paid over, and change paid
out, by cheques, I O U’s, and all sorts of queer
paper.
It was naturally a boon to the “ joey ’ -cheque
trade. Smart, gentlemanly fellows travelled all
over the Gulf and lived on the fat of the land on
cheques drawn on well-known firms. If the
public wouldn’t cash one, they would remark
airily—“ Well, what cheque will you take?”
“ Old Slinkura’s.” “Good.”
Then they would slip out to their camp—or, as
the term was, their saddle —and return noncha-
lantly bringing back a cheque on Slinkum uc.

MORTGAGED SQUATTER : “Wo finished lamb-marking at the station
last week, and I regret to say the increase is not up to last
year’s percentage.”
HAUGHTY BANKAII (snappishly): “How do you account for that?
Those dammed unions, I suppose.”
with just-written ink !
I’nbby would t. kc it, giving many cheques,
T O U’s., &c., in return, having to put up
with the scornful rejection of certain cheques, as
“not being good enough to pass along the road
we're going.”
Those cheques would be for months in circula-
tion at times, most old “pinoneers” knowing in
which direction to get rid of the joeys. Bill
Jumps was a “pinoneer.”
# • •
But the longest-tailed cat stands the worst
chance with a smart dog, and it happened some-
how that a parcel of Bill’s joeys got astray.
Notice was long in those days, and the arm of the
law slow and short in the Gulf.
» # •
Kiptown had two sudden sensations. A
black-tracker had got “bushed.” And Bill Jumps
had vanished.
Swiper Joe, Jumps's best bar-customer, vowed
he’d find Bill. (And he did.)
Other search-parties went out, but could pick
up no tracks. Swiper Joe played a lone hand.
One day Swiper came back in a fiercer sweat
than customary, and swore he had got the biggest
gatur that had ever kept a private boarding-house
in Jumper Ant Creek. He had shut it clean
through the neck and liver, and he guessed it
would float in a day or two.
“ What about Bill .1 umps ? ”
“Oh, I’ve given him best, an’ T aint agoin to
lose a 2;l-ft crockydile’s pelt for no wandering
delirium tremens cove.”
So the hoys started out to Jumper Ant Creek to
fetch in the orocky.
“ lie seems rather bulgy,” says Ben Blinks.
“’Spect his dinner disagreed with him,” says
Swiper, “or else he’s swole up since I gave him a
change of diet. Here goes to explore his iu’ards. ”
“Gosh ! It’s a man !,”
“Them’s Bill Jumps’s leggin’s, I’ll swear,” says
Swiper. “ That’s his cabbage-tree. Well, of all
the you’d a thought if the ’gatur could have
digested Bill Jumps’s carcase, he wouldn’t be
baulked on his leggings.’’
The body was undoubtedly that of the lost Bill
Jumps. Fragments of his clothing were distinctly
identified. His skull was almost intact and
various bones remained. Many a drunken sob
went up to heaven from Swiper Joe, who swore
by all his living jingoes and holy frosts and other
deities that the remains of Bill should have decent
burial—and Christian burial, too, for “if ever I
seen a Christian who could pass a joey cheque
Bill was the out an’-ontest.”
“ Where’s his pile?”
“Spect lie forwarded it to Sydney by last
month's steamer. There’s some pulp here that
looks like joeys, but I don’t see no shammy-leather
bag, with dust.”
Well, so it was. A parson buried Jumps, spoke
a long and stiring speech about his virtues, and
joined afterwards in the absolutely essential
whisky hooroar. And when a will was found,
making Swiper sole heir of Jumps’s possessions
and insurance money, why Swiper opened a free
bar all night, and the boys held a brandy-ball and
nearly burned and danced the humpy down.
# # *
Many years after, Benjamin, who had
meantime picked up a pile, returned from a
“ tower ” round the world, and was duly welcomed
by Swiper Joe, now a J.P. and with a strong
political chance of becoming an M.P. and even a
Minister of the Crown. (As a matter of fact, he
only reached one of the Australian Legislative
Councils, giving Bananaland best).
“ Look yere, Joe. I’m blest if I did not meet
Bill Jumps in London. Him as the halligator
yanked. Him as left you his money. It gave me
an orful turn. I thought it was his ghost. But I
bails him up, and he took it all right, and we
liquored up together, and it was Jumps.”
Swiper Joe gave a heavenly wink : “They do
say as ghosts rise from the dead sometimes.”
“Oh, stow that. What was the fake, Joe?”
“Do you remember the black tracker what was
lost, same time as Jumps cleared ?”
“ You don’t say?” said Ben, excitedly.
“Well, the blackfellers says that they ‘jump
up whitefellers ’ when they die. But that’s the
first time I knowed a blackfellow to ‘jump up’ a
white carkiss inside a crockydile ! And to think
we gave the carkiss Christian burial!”
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Jack Blackham, ever since he dined at the
famous Izaak Walton Inn, in Derbyshire, has
devoted his leisure to angling, either in the Yana
or Port Phillip Bay. The other day he got
chatting with another fisherman who didn’t know
him. Cricket turned up, of course. “Glad
Bruce is doing well,” said the stranger ; “by the
way, what’s become of old Jack Blackham; I don’t
see his name figuring now.” “ Oh, he’s got a bad
hand,” volunteered John McCarthy B. “Has
he? Sorry to hear that.” “Do you know him ?”
“What I Know old Jack Blackham? Hah!
that’s pretty good ! Ha, ha, ha? Why every-
body knows old Jack Blackham. I'd know him a
mile off.” This fairly “ stumped ” Jack, who says
he’s generally “ recognised ” at much shorter
range than a mile.
Disease ci'eeps Upon us Unawares, like an
assassin in the dark, and whose dangerous
proximity we never suspect until it makes the last
fatal clutch On some vital organ. We are always
warned in ample time of the impending danger,
but with criminal carelessness neglect these warn-
ings. That tired feeling, those aching limbs, and
that grand feeling one day and seedy condition
the next, the sour taste on waking in a moaning,
and the frequent sick and splitting headaches all
make their debiU before serious illness sets in.
All or any of these symptoms indicate the approach
of disease; they are faithful signs that the liver
and kidneys are not doing their duties, that the
morbid and effete matter, instead of being
eliminated from the system, is being retained, and
is positively poisoning and destroying the whole
physical structure. Neglect in such cases is
criminal. Recourse to rational treatment should
b ■ had at once. Clements Tonic should he taken
to strengthen the digestion, purify and fortify the
blood, to stimulate the liver, and brace up the
kidneys, to resolve and eliminate the poisonous
urea. Li ver and kidney complaints are the most
prevalent diseases of this country, and so long as
we consume such large quantities of animal food
and condiments, and drink so freely of tea and
stimulants, so long will this unhealthy condition
last. It is this mode of life that causes such
numbers of deaths from heart disease, Bright’s
disease, dropsy, Cancer, inflammation and enlarge*
ment of the liver, and similar causes, all of which
herald their approach by feelings of lassitude,
headache, languor, &c., and if prompt treatment
at once is adopted by a regular use of Clements
Tonic, the progress of disease is arrested, the
stomach, liver and kidneys resume their normal
action, and the poisonous accumulations are
expelled the system ,and normal health is restored.
That Clements Tonic is reliable is proved beyond
all question, and we have grateful beneficiaries in
every town and village who are conli mally
writing us in terms as did Mark Brody, Esq.,
High-st., Frankston, who writes :—“1 can assure
you nothing will give me greater pleasure than to
let the public- know what Clements Tonic has
done for Mrs. Brody. When a girl about 17 years
of age she caught a severe cold, which turned to
bronchitis, and for two years after she suffered
with nervous exhaustion accompanied by head-
ache, neuralgia and lowness of spirits. Several
doctors were consulted in the matter, some saying
one thing, some another. One stated that one
lung was completely gone and the other badly
affected. No. 2 said she was suffering from a
complication of diseases, and treated her for an
affection of the heart. Ever since then up till
about two months ago (and her age is now lu) she
has never been free from headache, or some of the
general ailments accompanied by the same low-
ness of spirit. About two months ago she tried
Clements Tonic, and finding that she received
some relief I procured more, and, now, to-day she
stands a living witness of the wonders it will
work, for no one that saw her a year ago would
think it was the same woman. Her appetite has
returned, and she has no need for help to do her
work, and, what is better, she is in good spirits.
I have recommended it to several, who, I know,
are using it with good results, and I shall continue
to make it known as much as possible.—Yours
very truly, Mark Brody, High-street, Frankston,
Victoria.”

Come to the Domain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cone to the Domain of “spouting,”
Come on Sunday after dinner,
That’s the time when men are shouting
Theories for saint and sinner.
After dinner saint and sinner get their notions
knocked about;
Reason strikes a new idea, Logic kicks an old one
out.
Come and hear the declarations
Bold with rhetoricians’ charms,
Floored by stormy contradictions,
Shaking fists and swaying arms.
Declaration, Contradiction, “ Yon maintain” and
“I deny,”
That’s the —social engine squeaking for some
lubrication—cry.
Come and hear the kind abstainers
Solve the myst’ry of the Sphinx,
And the Sydney tumbler-drainers •
Rather swallow it—in drinks.
Kind abstainers, telling drainers, “ Wine is mis’ry,
shame, and blood,”
Too forgetful that things were not quite the thing
before the Flood.
Come to the Domain and listen
To the cant of “ would-be ” teachers;
Come and see the tear-drop glisten
In the eyes of earnest preachers—
“ Would-be” teachers, earnest preachers, telling
sinners all about
How to get to certain places when they’ve pegged
their six feet out.
Come and see new speakers shaping—
Just emerging from the shell —
Come and twig the youngsters gaping
At the arguments on Hell— _ .
Speakers shaping, youngsters gaping, superstition
going “bung,” _
As irrev’rent jokers jeer it in the presence ot the
young.
See the Single Tacks projecting
(When our revenue grows less
By prohibitive protecting)
We will tax the land. I guess !
Their projection needs protection, but their points
have scratched the hand
Of protecting friendship proffered from the
foreign-trading band.
Come to the Domain of “ mashing,”
Come on Sunday afternoon,
Come when creed with creed is clashing
Cymbal-like to any tune.
Lovers mashing, bigots clashing, scenes of plea-
sure, themes of pain,
Ev’ry afternoon on Sunday, out in Sydney’s green
Domain.

The Prince of Wales has no reason to
doubt for one moment that “ my colonial friends ’
take the wannest interest in the Imperial Insti-
tute, but he wants them to pay up, “My
colonial! ”
The Argus would have Freetrade in
brains, apparently. Its columns are open to the
drivel of one “ H.H.L.,” who heaps scorn upon
the Protectionist, Sam Lister, of Bradford, for
having fought “lawsuit after lawsuit to prevent
anyone using or infringing his patent improve-
ments in wool-combing.” Lister’s motto is
“ Monopoly,” screams the Argus critic. Every
inventor tries to be a monopolist of his own in-
ventions. Yea, from the most liberal-minded
bard or novelist down to the author of Guinness’
Stout.
The Bulletin has not been uniformly
kind and considerate to Parkes, but it has never
allowed a correspondent to “ dedicate ” to the old
man such awful doggerel as was lately printed in
a prominent portion of the Age. Two verses :
•• Wool and iron ” bought abroad,
That is all Foreign earning.
We want to make them all at Home,
And that's for what we’re yearning.
Importers, foreign ship-owners,
For F’reetrade all are howling ;
But we stand by Home Industry,
Home Mining and Home Farming.
Parkes is no doubt shaky in his political principles
and poetry, but lie’s altogether a nobler intellect
than the man who attempted to crush him with
that rhyme “ Per favour of the Age.”
Dear Bulletin’, — I came across the Xmas
number of a cockney publication (the Lady’s
Pictorial, or something) the other day, and read
therein a yarn which had been advertised as the
“Special Xmas Story” for the Australasian.
Enquiring further into this coincidence I found
that the “special "attraction had been “secured,”
not written, for the. Australasian, and then it
occured to me that the agricultural organ was
trying to tighten the silken bonds that bind, &c.,
Ac. How would one of these knock about stories
1,,.’“ secured ? ” And how many different papers,
pray, loaded up their sparkling Xmas Number
with this same heavy lump of literature? —Yours,
Melbourne.
At Port Melbourne Police Court the other
day, a Norwegian seaman was fined 10s. and costs
for assaulting a lady aboard ship. The lady stated
that she. was endeavouring to stop the accused tar
from stonshing the first mate on the high seas,
when the heartless ruffian kicked heron the breast.
Against this sad story a crowd of witnesses de-
posed that the lady had backed up the first mate,
whipped off her petticoat and proclaimed herself
“an American tighter” thirsting for the blood of
the accused. When she tried to scratch his eyes
out, the vile, unmanly creature had pushed her
away. This case should evidently have been
decided strictly on the petticoat question. When
a lady deliberately takes off her underwear and
prepares to give beans to the world at large, she
can't claim the usual allowance for sex in a police
court. The muddle-headed beaks of Port Mel-
iKiurne ought to have stood upon the petticoat and
delivered a decided judgment one way or the
other. Either this was a shamefully-used lady, or
a rampageous female who deserved to pay five
“ bob ” to the poor-box for getting a black eye.

He Didn’t Anticipate Trouble.
AN amorous couple on the verge of committing
matrimony entered a Paddington bus the
other night, and oblivious of the presence of a
third pa«senger, who to all appearance was enjoy-
ing a comfortable nap in the top corner,
commenced billing and cooing in dead earnest.
Of course the conversation turned upon the sub-
ject of their approaching nuptials, and many were
the expressed anticipations of the joy that awaited
them in that estate which all are anxious to enter
into and not a few would be glad to obtain release
from. “ Ah,” whispered the fair one, suppressing
a mild, hysterical sob, “ what a pleasure it will be
for me to listen to all your troubles and share all
your griefs.” “ But, darling,” rejoined the hope-
ful Romeo, “ I have no griefs nor troubles.
‘■Oh, but you will have plenty, you know, when
we are married,” added she, with ingenuous
emphasis. The passenger in the corner coughed,
and a silence followed that would have tipped
down the scale at 300 pounds. The prospective
benedict assumed a thoughtful mood. Cp to that
moment he had regarded marriage as an unmixed
blessing—a path strewn with roses—not even
once pausing to think of the discomfort attending
a pair of feminine cold feet, of an icy reception
after a late night at the lodge or club, of a colicky
baby with leathern lungs and a voice like the
shrillest of steam whistles, or of bruised and
lacerated fingers, the combined result of unskil-
fulness, a blunt tomahawk, and a chunk of knotty
firewood. These, however, are among the little
troubles that the average young man undertaking
one of the great responsibilities of life must look
oat for, and the best thing for him to do is to
scothe the self-inflicted injuries of inexperience,
and to prevent the attack of chill or rheumatism
which shooting by night at lightning speed out
of a warm bed, and rushing wildly along linoleum •
covered passages in quest of paregoric will in-
evitably bring, is always to have in the house
ready for use a supply of St. Jacobs Oil, the great
conqueror of all pain. Mr. John Breakwell, or
Milson’s Point-road, North Sydney, gratefully
acknowledges : “I have suffered severely tor
some years from rheumatism in my knees, and
have tried many remedies. I have found St.
Jacobs Oil gives me great relief, and am pleased
to speak in praise of it.”
