846 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, Jan. 4, 1893,
My Dear Moorabinda,—
I wonder what shades of difference the society scribess
who keeps her eagle eye on gilded events, descries in the
various weddings that one is “a pretty wedding,”
another “an interesting wedding” (1 remember one
weird paragraph alluding to a wedding as an “ interesting
event!”), a third a “solemn ceremony,” etcetera! To
the average onlooker one wedding is much the same as
the other, except that the bridesmaids are a telescopic
quantity and the bridegroom is sometimes a shade too
brilliant in the colour of his trousers.
It is always safe to speak of a marriage as “interest-
ing”—all women find it so—accordingly that current
adjective doesn’t need explaining. The average woman’s
notion of a “pretty wedding” is one where the bride is
spliced under a floral bell and spruce little girls throw
rose-leaves at her as she marches her victim to the white-
horsed carriage. And I suppose a solemn ceremony is
the dreary sacrifice where the bride is 45 and says “ I
will ” like the snap of a trigger, and the bridegroom
boards with her ma?
There are very few girls possessed of a big or little
brother who don’t sometimes fervently wish that he or
they had never been born. Brothers are the fly in many
a sister’s ointment—but it is fortunately only seldom that
one of them gives his sister away on her marriage-morn,
as happened at a Sydney wedding recently. At the break-
fast he rose to propose the bride’s health. “ I hope,” ha
said, “that my sister will be very happy—but I can assure
the bridegroom that he will be a lucky man if she sews on
a button for him. She never put a stitch in for .mb in her
life.” Then he satdown with the solemn air of a youth
who had done his duty, and the bride suddenly found
that she had only five minutes left to catch the train in.
A N.S. W. squatter’s bride had a dreadful experience
recently through dressing in a hurry. She had got into
the white robes of sacrifice at express speed—bolted into
the buggy and rushed off to the townghip church where
an admiring mob awaited her, and it was only' when she
commenced a blushing descent that she remembered she
hadn’t.changed her boots. A pair of house-boats—l mean
bools—loomed, hoof-like, from beneath her snowy dra-
peries, and the only redeeming feature was that the
bridegroom wasn’t looking.
No woman, “under any pretext whatever” (as the
theatrical managers say when they want to stem the tide
of dead-heads) should allow herself to be rushed over her
dressing on her wedding-day. For no well-regulated
bride with a proper regard for the unities could be seen
taking in a reef with her mouth full of pins when once she
arrived at the church—she has got to go through with (he
thing on her merits even if her veil is hanging by one
corner and her collar choking her.
It has taken me a week to get over my astonishment at
at the dazzling splendours of Sydney Lyceum Theatre,
where blue and yellow plush have it all th ir own impres
sionist way in the dress-circle, and a blue plush curtain of
fabulous price hangs in a golden frame. I don’t like the
drop—l’m not in sympathy with painted palaces, and
gondolas, and marionette figures in cloaks. And I’m sure
the youth who squires a hen-party doesn’t like the dainty
salons opening on to a fern-shaded balcony which are
delightful features in the new play-house, built with
extravagant roominess everywhere. The youth—as I was
saying—won’t like the salons, because you can get ice-
cream and other lady-loved refreshments there—which
means another saveloy lunch for Gussie after every time
he takes Tottie to the play. Sydney Lyceum is a very
society edifice, and I hope local femininity will endeavour
to dress up t) it, and not sneak in looking as if they had
done their love-locks in the dark, and had thrown them-
selves sartorially on the mercy of a home-made cashmere
opera cloak—as they sometimes used to at the “Royal.”
When a lavish management poises you in a golden chair,
and conducts you thither on a golden carpet, the least
you can do is to use the clothes-brush vigorously before
starting.
“Little Red Riding Hood”—the Lyceum’s first-born—
does spectacular credit to her environment, and although
the attire of some of the girls makes them seem eligible
for a vote for thrift, there is no niggardly economy in
their display of beauty. Alice Leamar, in the name-part,
doesn’t change her grey skirt (even pantomime virtue is
expressed by a mousey tint) and red hood until the last
act, when she looks like a wedding bon-bon in white
brocade and silver, set off by absorbing pink tights and
silver meshes with plum >d hat en suite, worn by Ad.lie
Conyers when Red Riding Hood and Hoy Blue pose so
confidingly in the big gold wedding-ring of the transforma-
tion scene.
The management have done full justice to Miss Conyers
ftn d clothed her in marvellous blue tights, and not in
martial scarlet and stage mail, which the strapping Addie
carries superbly. Cora Tinnie, more garrulous than the
Gaiety people ever let her be, looks like the girl who gels
painted on the posters, in doublet and trunks of yellow
and black with fur round the collar and effective string of
diamonds—and Courtneidge’s Watteau jacket in blanket
dimmed with green ribbons is a lovely smack at the
oolish virgin who patronises Cheap Jack for what can
on 'y he cut by a tailor.
The first day of Tattersall’s New Year meet at Rand-
J'ick was spoilt by rainy, unsettled weather; only one
society girl appeared on the Mcmbers’-stand, like one
"ok in a thunder-storm.
Racecourses seemingly can’t help being haunts of
o gory. Somebody told me the simple story of a puhli-
’an who wanted to dispense heady liquids at an Austra-
mn ra ce-meeting (not to be too particular) and who
PPlied to the secretary for the right of way, or whatever
it la, to the grand-stand bar—and got it. “But,” said
the secretary, “you must have an assured brand of stuff
in there. Give the outside people what you like, but we
can t have ’em poisoned in the paddock." “Of course
thers is a higher charge,” said the hotel-man, but the
secretary calmly replied, “Oh, no—same price all over
the course !’’ This paragraph ought to be as good as a
temperance-lecture to the shilling seats.
The unexpected showers and shine on the second day of
Tattersall s Club meeting were in lovely harmony with
the running. The noticeable thing about Members’-stand
style was a marked tendency to black picked out with
something that caught the eye. Mrs. W. Mitchell wore a
trained black woollen with yoke and cuffs of gold-and-
black bullion embroidery ; little bonnet to match. Mrs.
Burdekin wore an involved garment of black, “ luck,
pointed (as the architects say) with green ; sma'i
bonnet en suite. A well-known society girl wore
a grandmotherly gown of black silk and lace, which
made her look like her married sister, who wore
a mulberry vest with an Eton suit and a rough
straw’ hat simply trimmed with a bow of prune velvet.
Miss Myra Kemble sported a charming get-up of biscuit-
coloured silky stuff with yoke and sleeves of old-gold silk
covered with guipure embroidery, the bodice cut seamless
after the French fashion, the skirt finished with an old-
gold silk frill; black grenadine hat trimmed with black
and yellow’. Miss Rankin wore a black gown flowered in
mauve with front of green silk, neat hat in black jet and
touch of green. A very swagger frock was of stone-
coloured silk (worn by a Queensland lady) which measured
the ultra-fashioned nine yards round the hem, and was
tiimmed on the bodice with emerald-green velvet—but
the laurel-w reath of splendour must go to a lady in copper-
coloured silk punctuated with large bunches of mauve
and green and red and white flowers at the rate of one
every now and then. This neat thing in upholstery was
w orked up to parrokeet pitch by rows of scarlet gimp and
topped off by a black hat heavily trimmed w'th mauve.
The lady was appropriately built, of course. Whenever a
substantial siren catches sight of a pattern that can be
heard above the traffic she invariably plunges in and pur-
chases 15 yards.
There must be a lot of strange people come to Sydney
for the New Year. It is advertised that a “lizardskin
lady s purse has heen lost. A lizardskin lidy is quite a
new departure in girls—even at pantomime time. I sup-
pose her name is “Gohanna.”
Apropos of nothing, it’s not only Sir George Dibhs who
feels Ike anathematizing Chicago. The latest apple of
discord is that somebody wants to go, officially, and pay
her own footing, and considers it a cheap way of getting
at all the privileges appertaining to the position, while
somebody else is desperately anx'ous to go, but wants to
have her way paid for her out of the Treasury. Looks
simple enough on paper - but there isn’t a phonograph in
the world big enough to hold all the talkee-talkie.
The Madame Soldene benefit concert in Sydney Towr,
Hall on New Year’s Eve attracted a satisfactory audience
especially in the popular part of the building, but, taking
the programme as a whole, a mob of two deaf mutes anc
an imbecile w’ould have been the most suitable audience
fw the occasion. The “Lohengrin” trio (Wiegand,
“Pussy” Poussard and Hu norbein); the “Faust” duo
for organ and piano (Wiegand and Miss Griffith), and
Poussard’s “Scotch Fantasia,” were soothing instru-
mentally, and Madame Soldene’s neat phrasing and
artistic singing in “ 1 Love Him So Mr. Farley’s “ Les
Rameaux and perhaps Mr. VValshe’s “Love is a Dream”
(though it might have been “ Love is a very, very hard-
boiled egg," for all the fervour he put into his singing
voice), w’ere the only vocal items in a bill nineteen shrieks
long.
Miss Ada Colley, in white satin and tulle, with very
fair hair, dressed in spangled bands, cut a charming
figu’-e on the platform, and her childish voice rang
shrilly in “The Little Birds,” but the rest of the amateurs
were badly dressed and very raw, and turned the .show-
in to a pupils’ concert. Kowalski, by the way, was billed
to assist, but, owing to an accident at the last moment,
couldn’t appear.
Apropos concerts, it’s a strange spectacle to see the
extremely undressed lady at the piano accentuate in her
person the throbbings and gaspings intended to convey
deep feeling in the prima donna. If our songstress is
drawing her breath in agonized gasps that set the paste
necklace gyrating like a ship at sea the lady at the wheel
is seen to be billowing o’er with emotion at the same
time. And when joy comes at last to the singer a beatific
smile is seen to break all over the accompanist’s visage
too. You see something like it when an impressionable
girl goes to the play and reproduces, as she watches him,
all the grimaces of the funny man.
That was indeed an awkward accident which happened
to M. Kowalski. He had hurt his finger, and, on the
advice of a friend, applied some spirit to it. A few
moments afterwards he happened to light a match, and
the flame caught the bandage—resulting in a terrible
burn which, it was for a time feared, might have for ever
silenced him as a pianist.
The latest soapy story of a Southern hotel, where all
the bath-rooms have partitions which end before they
reach the ceiling, concerns a barrister and a prima donna
much addicted to whistling. By standing on the edge of
the bath one bather might observe the other, and as the
occupant of the next compartment was whistling like a
bird, the barrister(not thinking, of course, that the siffleur
was a woman), mounted nimbly on the edge of his tub
and popped his head over the partition. “ Would you
please lend me the soap?” he said, politely—but he didn’t
wait to get it. The vision of a charming nymph in the
bathing-costume of the year One. made him drop off his
perch like a bird hit by a catapult.
I have received from Mr. J. Franca a copy of the
“ Nithsdale Schottische, played with great success by
Need’s Band.” The schottische is a not particularly tune-
ful composition, but the beat is as regular as that of a
piston-rod, and that is really all one wants. You may
like a melting tune with trimmings but the machine-made
article, played on a barrel-organ, is the most satisfactory
dance-music, after all.
Bondi (N.S.VV.) Aquarium was dominated by N.Q.
aboriginals last Saturday—the crowd that Mr. Me-ilon has
got together for exhibition in Chicago. Mr. M. made an
inaudible lecture from the platform, an 1 wore his nether
garments turned up at the hem ; and I do hope he will
orate better in America, where the stump orator, in
dtzzling store clothes, has been brought to an aitonishing
pitch of voluble audibility.
It was very interesting to see the aboriginals throw the
boomerang, slide the snakey “ducoon” through the
grass—spear a sheet of bark and climb up and down the
switchback supports like monkeys. They are firm-look-
ing men, with skins like well washed potatoes, sparsely
decorated with white war paint. The beau of the lot was
a well-knit youth primitively got up in a pair of blanket
trunks and a bunch of gaudy feathers on each arm, and
he was run pretty close in the matter of abbreviated style
by a gentleman with a red flannel band round his skull.
The king was there attired in all the cold i.dendour of a
bead necklace, and so was the queen. Her sable
Majesty’s crop of tightly-curled black wool was the very
apotheosis of a civilised bang.
One popular thing Mayor Manning has done is to erect
a much-needed portico over the front steps of Sydney
Town Hall. On wet evenings the flight from your vehicle
to the mat was a terribly sloppy experience, with a two-
to-one chance that you might have to turn and go home
again with a dress all draggled round the frill. 1 saw a
girl fall on the slippery steps one evening, splash all the
front of her dress, and go away in tears, and many a hang
has hung dismally into its owner’s eyes because of that
unprotected entrance.
Yours affectionately,
SATTHO SMITH.
The Wild-Cat Column.
Yalwal has always been the N.S.W. mining
terra incognita. Where, on earth, is Yalwal?” is often
asked, and in nine cases out of ten the conundrum is
unanswered. Yet the biggest battery in N.S.W. is there
Bound, of 50 heads. There Is another
Of 40 heads, the Pioneer, and, what is more important,
both are kept going night and day. What they turn out
the pubhc never know. Also, as a matter of course,
both mines are owned and managed by Victorians. Both
are worked by open cuts, and the ore consists of soft slate
and other mullock, intersec'ed with small veins and fibres
of quartz rich in gold, but whether the body, crushed,
goes one dwt. or an oz ,we have no information. If the
mines were in Victoria the owners would be compelled to
publish their returns. Why not in N.S.W. ? A small
N.S. W. syndicate, inoluding John Newton, has a lease at
the foot of the hill, on which the Homeward Bound and
rmneer are situated, and have driven a tunnel over 4Coft.
mto the hill to strike the same foundation at a depth,
i i? 6 j ounc i nothing, and are now within 30ft. of the
boundary of the Pioneer 00. Their only hope is to rise
above the tunnel.

One Alfred Jeffery palmed off an
“original” story on Cassels, the London pub-
lishers. Unfortunately for him, Cassels submitted
it to the man who had written and published it a
couple of years before. Jeffery was arrested and
committed for false pretences.
The S.A. Post and Telegraph Office, the
worst managed institution of its kind this side
of the line, has managed to burn 30 letters and
half destroy scores of others in a Krishmish
fumigation. Todd, C.M.G., has too much to do.
tie is Postmaster-General, Superintendent of
Telegraphs, and Government Astronomer, while
he fills in spare time by making hideous puns and
attending Conferences in the adjacent provinces.
Sydney Truth (now conducted by our old
comrade, Traill) remarks that the best resource of
Miss Shaw, the Times commissioner, would
probably have been to hunt up Sir Samuel
Griffith’s own speeches on the Kanaka question,
of some years back, as a politician and as a
criminal lawyer. “We understand that Miss
Shaw must shortly return to London. She ex-
plained recently that she absolutely must get
back, because a twelve months’ absence would
cause her to lose all the threads of events at
home. Now, Miss Shaw has been absent from
these colonies longer than that. She has been
absent all her life, yet she professes to have
gathered and unravelled the most tangled clues of
Australian affairs within a few months.
To judge by a verdict given in N.S.W.
District Court, in re electioneering expenses
alleged to have been incurred in Hawkesbury
constituency on behalf of that inarticulate politi-
cal dunderhead millionaire, Burdekin, M.P., the
N.S.W. electoral laws need a little attention in
respect to definitions as to “undue influence.”
The plaintiff declared that Burdekin's general
instructions were : “Do for me as you did for
Mr. Bowman’’—the late member—“and I shall
be very well satisfied ; use your own dis-
cretion and be liberal.” In consequence of
which, the plaintiff “ Look buggies and went cut
canvassing, . . . he shouted freely. He
generally took a bottle of whisky with him when
he went out canvassing. Plaintiff was outside
scrutineer on the polling-day, and lie spent some
■£S in drinks on that day.” The plaintiff sued for
-034 18s. ; the defendant paid ,010 into Court,
but said in the witness-box that he was willing to
pay what Honor thought was a fair thing.
Thereupon the Judge thought 04 more, with -01
4s. costSj and the expenses of two witnesses, was
* fair thing.

affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried several
Doctors and many Remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas,

PURELY HERBAL “ UNIVERSAL ALP ALINE
PILLS,” which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare
COMPLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and
■although this is over five years ago, I have remained well ever
since.
THE ALE ALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
khis solemn declaration, conscientiously believing the same to
be true.
THOS. MUDIMAN.

November, 1891, before me,
S. T. Whiddon, Justice of the Peace.

Supreme Court of New South Wales and Victoria.

A Game of Euchre.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I once spent tliree days with Honi Wetere Te
Rerenga ( Anrjlice , John Wesley the Snag) at his
principal village on the Mokau (M.L.)
The rest of the party wanted to see the snow-
crowned summit of Ruapehu at short range; I had
a sore heel and was principally interested in the
problem of how to quickly cultivate some
epidermis thereon.
Three days with a cannibal chief just pardoned
after 20 years’ outlawry for murder ! That’s an
experience anyway.
First we played euchre ; a hundred of potatoes
on the game and a pig on the rubber! Wetere
had his greenstone mere or war-club beside him.
“ What him for?” I asked in pigeon English.
“S’pose you cheat!” replied the boss Ngati-
maniapoto.
I presume that he noticed a slight tremor of
fear on my face as I put down “ the Dutchman’s
point,” for he said, “ Always the whole time long
when I play euchre and a taurikarika (slave) and
him cheat, crack him skull.” Then he paused,
spat into the ovm of stones, and added, senten-
tiously, “ No taurikarika in this kainga cheat me
now.”
I played fair and owed Wetere three sacks of
potatoes and a pig.
Then we tried Yankee grab.
“ Kia kaha,” said the chief, as he set a main of
eighteen for the fourth time running.
“ What’s that in English, Wetere?” I asked.
“ Oh 1 It mean * Be plenty strong all the time,’ ”
said he.
“ Well, Wetere, you’re too jolly strong for me!’
said I, picking up a “ tat ” all fives and sixes that
he had rung in. “ Where you get him ?”
“ Kimball Bent make him for me. You no tell
Mitchelson ?’
‘‘No, Wetere, I won’t tell Mitchelson.”
“Kapai; then you no pay me potatoes and
pigs?”
“No, Wetere ! Bet your life I won’t.”
And that’s the last time I gambled with a
gentleman who played with a greenstone club
within arm’s length.
J. A. PHILP.
A prominent N.Q. civil servant has been
getting himself disliked a good deal of recent
years. He is an old, reliable drunkard, and is
related to a well-known politician. He held a
billet for some time in a town near the Gulf, but
when he began to run about the streets in an
advanced state of undress, the inhabitants peti-
tioned almost unanimously for his removal. He
was removed accordingly— to a better billet in
another town, with a handsome rise of salary. He
celebrated this promotion with some more
wild orgies, in the course of which he
had to be carried home on several occa-
sions ; also, he tried to run amok in the
town hall, fought several rounds with the door-
keeper, manifested a desire to commit suicide
with a razor, and delivered one or two thrilling
lectures on snakes in the principal street of the
town. Then he settled down in a saturnine mood
in his office, and when anybody called on business
he either told him briefly to go to perdition, or
else informed him that he was of illegitimate des-
cent and that his mother eloped with a Chinaman.
At latest advices he still holds the fort manfully—
except when be comes out and rushes after some-
body with a saw and the inhabitants are
petitioning unsuccessfully for his removal.
Advocates of Woman’s Rights,” remarks
an American writer, “ must not seek in Renan’s
writings for arguments to support their cause.
For all his admiration of woman, he does not
desire to see her moulding herself after the male
model. He only admires womanly women ; he
has no words strong enough to condemn the blue-
stocking. Indeed, be loVes her so well that ho
loves her faults, so long as they are womanly
faults. Thus he says :■ ‘ The woman who most
resembles ns is antipathetic. What we look for in
the other sex- is the opposite of ourselves. Weak-
ness, false reasoning, narrow ideas, ignorance,
superstition, shock us in a man; they make us
smile in a woman. We like to see a graceful hand
make the sign of the cross. Narrow duties ought
to have corresponding narrow ideas. Faith in
woman is a virtue, it must be respected alike with
all feminine virtues. It is a mistake to believe
that we are desirous of bringing women round to
our philosophical opinions. On the contrary, we
are often glad they will not listen to us. We
admire their preconceived determination not to
listen to what might weaken their heroic resolu-
tion. I should be grieved to have in any way
assisted in diminishing piety in woman. Pietnx,
taken in its fine Latin sense, meaning tenderness
and weakness, is the excellent gift that has fallen
to their share. When I last went to Brittany, I
was happy to see that the young girls there were
as pleasing, as modest, as well brought up as 50
years ago. My one desire is that they shall go on
being so. I shall be consoled if I can know, after
my death, that women are as pretty and that love I
is as sweet as it used to be.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Ever since the lolanthe disaster in Sydney har-
bour all the loose cranks in N.S.W. have been writing to
the papers. One of them wants a regulation that no
women or children are to go out in sailing-boats. Another
wants a police launch to follow every sailing boat that
goes out and see that the people on board know how to
manage it—quite regardless of the fact that it would
require 150 police launches for the work, and that they
would probably run each other down in the confusion,
and that a policeman’s opinion on the subject doesn’t
amount to much anyhow. A third individual desires
that nobody should be allowed to go out till he
knows how to manage a boat—in other words, he
isn’t to go into the water till he can swim. A
fresh spectre proposes that every boat-owner should
put the man who hires a boat through an examination,
and if he can’t pass he shouldn’t have the boat. A violent
crank writes to say that the people who hire boats are no
good ; it is only the capitalist who has a boat of his own
who can be trusted on the water. Somebody else sends
in a verse of a hymn, and draws attention to Thessalonians,
iv., 13-8 ; I Corinthians, xv. ; and Romans, x., 9-10 ; also,
another party wants jibing abolished by Actof Parliament,
and thinks the main-sheet shouldn’t be more than two
feet over the wash-board. The rest are convinced that no
sailing boat should be allowed to go out except in a dead
calm. The only practical suggestion is that the sail-area
of hired boats carrying women and children should be
minimised. Every additional few feet of mast means tons
more weight on a boat’s gunwale.

Chatham’s Sydney Tattersall’s Cup was the

What has become of that sterling hurdler,
Bound ? He won a couple of races and then mysteriously
dieippeared from the scene of his triumphs.
• * •
Glowworm’s “ stable ” must have had an anxious
few seconds at the finish of the Nursery Handicap at
Randwick on Saturday, Pearl Powder, her stable com-
panion, led until within a few yards of the post, and the
Ally they wanted to win with only just got her nose in
front in time.
# # •
Jockey Death rode favourite Idle Boy to victory
in the Steeplechase, at Randwick, on Saturday, where-
upon one of the books remarked that it was very evident
the joker who wrote “Death, where is thy sting?”
hadn’t fielded against the favourite in the Steeplechase.
• • #
“A. J. Miller,” the person who advertises a
sweep in Sydney, gave as the winner of second prize
(£4,275), in his “combined Melbourne and Caulfield Cup
consultation,” the name of “A. D. M'Andrews, Thursday
Island, Q.” Thursday Island is a small place, and the
local police, “after a searching enquiry,” can find no such
person there.
• # •
One of the chief morals of the recent boating-
disasier is that no deep-water sailormen appear to know
anything about harbour boats.
# * #
The Newcastle, N.S.W., guessing-game is run
by one “ Agricola.” The last task he set his credulous
victims was to form, from the letters “ Esnaerl,” two real
English words to be found in the dictionary. The first
prize was £5O, and the tariff, a shilling a guess. The two
English words (which, of course, no one guessed) were
Ellen Sara. This humbug has a standing advertisement
in the local paper which must cost him at least £3 a
week.
• • •
The bowl-players of Australia have nearly all
reduced themselves to that sad condition by what is
known as the flowing bowl.
• # •
They don’t want imported “ spielers ” in Maori-
land. A Sydney racecourse crowd went over there the
other day, and ere many days the police had swept ’em
all into gaol for terms of from three months up. When
they got to quod they were going to “see Maoriland in
blazes before they worked,” so the gaoler sent for the
magistrate, who put them on bread and water.
» » •
A falling-off in the racing crowd at Caulfield
(Melb.) on Boxing Day was explained, in the stupid
“great" papers, by the “large number of counter
attractions, the most powerful of all being, of course, the
intercolonial cricket match.” It is the rule to speak of
counter attractions at Xmas time as though they
never had to be reckoned with before, and were an
unlooked-for misfortune to the shows that don’t happen
to boom. The intercolonial match on Boxing Day is a
venerable fixture, older than the meeting at Caulfield.
• • •
Another week will be added to the harmless life
of Lord Sheffield when he hears that he has succeeded in
waking up Victorian cricket and reviving local interest in
the game. And when we remember that Sheffield
accomplished his mission at the expense of Melbourne
football, whioh began to decline last season, no one will
begrudge the kindly old gentleman his complacent
ohuokle.
• * •
In the Victorian eleven playing against N.S.W.,
nine of the men were noted club bowlers, although not
one—except, perhaps, M'Leod—had the slightest pretence
to be called a orack trundler.
• # •
At a recent race-meeting near Melbourne, the
winner of the selling race was ridden by a promising
lightweight, aged 78. He only won after a desperate
finish, too.
# # *
Wellington (M.L.) City Council won’t allow
liquor to he sold on any of the city reserves—the sports,
athletic, and cricket grounds.
• • •
The manager of the axemen’s contest at Mel-
bourne should have given additional variety to his
show by including paling-splitting and squaring, and
firewood-chopping engagements. From an onlooker’s
point, there is more fun in a paling-splitting mill than all
the rest together.
• • •
The difference between the two Victorian
opinions of Bannerman stonewalling against the local
team, and Aleck stonewalling against the Britishers is as
noticeable as ever. In the one case, the little man is
“sickening,” and in the other he’s a “ perfect treat.”
* * *
They produce some good galloways in the
Northern province. Kildare, a 14.2 pony, won the Ipswich
(Q ) Handicap on Boxing Day, beating a field of good
horses and cutting out the 10 furlongs in 2min. lliseo.,
almost equal to Cerise and Blue or Crown Jewel, both of
whom were under the galloway height.
« # *
A burglar pursuing his vocation in a Brisbane
suburb the other night “ woke up the wrong passenger.”
He entered the house of a noted amateur athlete, who
caught him red-handed. The burglar showed fight, but
was thrown in half-a-dozen ways in as many seconds,
promptly roped up, and handed over to the police in a
terribly damaged condition.
The brother of a professional tipster advertises
that he has joined the ranks of the gentle-voiced bookies.
Between them they should make it interesting for the
poor punter.
• * *
As the butcher’s boat neared the ship Oakland,
five miles off Newcastle, N.S.W., an individual, with the
frame of a 16mo. Hercules and the voice of a stentor,
lumped upon the rail and asked the boatmen who was
champeen fighter of Newcastle. The watermen yelled
back the name of a local pug., whereupon the sailor
vociferously announced that he would fight hbn for love
or mousy—principally mousy. When the tout* board..
the vessel, this belligerent seaman asked if they thought
he could make much money fighting in Australia. The
master-tout sarcastically informed him that the loser’s
end of a few purses would bring him in about a pound a
month, without, however, damping the bloodthirsty
ardour of the new arrival; for as soon as he got ashore,
he hied him to the boxing-hall and interviewed George
Powell. The next day he was informed two local
fighters were yearning to fight him. “ Well,” said he,
“ that kinder puts me in a fix. If I fight oae, the other
will probably say I’m afraid. I’ll have to knock ’em both
out in one night." He was serious, and the matches were
arranged in accordance with his wishes. He did not want
any training, and would fight any night. Black Sam
came on first, and after four-and-a-half rushing rounds,
the nigger breathlessly implored his seconds to “ chuck
up der -chair,” as he could not be expected to fight the
hind leg of a dray-horse. After a short spell the sailor
said he was ready for the next course, and Corrigan, a
powerful fellow, with some pretensions to science, was
pitted against him. In the tenth round the sailor suc-
ceeded in completely inco-ordinating his second opponent,
and thus fulfilled his self-imposed contract. The name of
this gluttonous man-batterer is Tom Ball.
# * *
The grand old pine, just inside the members’
gate on the M.C.C. ground, has been cut down at the
instance of one solitary cricketer who complained that
the tree “ swallowed ” the ball when he was at the
wicket, and affected his hatting eye. That pine has, for
years, been a favourite trysting-place for the collar-and-
cuff brigade and its girl. But “ the play’s the thing 1 "

* # #
The retirement from the cricket-field of one of
Australia’s most brilliant, popular, and consistent bats-
men is caused by a rare and intractable leg-injury which
has puzzled the surgeons.
* * *
On Summer Cup day at Randwick, while Gov.
Jersey was in the birdcage shaking hands, as is his wont,
with trainers and jockeys, he spoke to a prominent
trainer, at the time girthing a favourite, and extended
his hand. The trainer scarcely turned his head, and, in
his stooping position, gave vice-royalty the tips of the
fingers of his left hand.
• • #
Aboriginal Charley Samuels, ex-champion ped.,
appeared in the dock at Sydney Central during the holi-
days, charged with “ celebrating the occasion.” He is
but a phantom of his former self.
• * *
The record interprovincial cricket “ gate ”
doesn’t necessarily betoken better times at Melbourne.
It may mean that thousands of people no longer have
enough money to “ train it ” to the racecourse.
# * *
Otago, the “ sorehead ” province of Maoriland,
is always “ agin everything ” that does not originate in
Dunedin, and nothing ever does originate in Dunedin. It
was “agin ” the M.L. Masonic Grand Lodge, the Rowing
Association, and the Athletic Association, but had to give
in at the end. Now it is doing its best against the M.L.
Football Union, and will meet with a like fate, and for
three years the Otago delegates have prevented the for-
mation of a Maoriland Jockey Club. As the rest of the
province favours it, however, the racing men now mean to
follow the example of Hoben, in the Football Union, and
form without Otago, and if necessary, disqualify Otago
horses from running elsewhere.
* # *
Pavilion chat said that Sammy Jones was to be
rewarded with £5O by his father if he made 50 runs in the
second innings N.S.W. v. Victoria. He only reached 45.

# * *
Ooningham was the solitary source of amuse-
ment in the N.S.W. v. Victoria cricket-match, last week,
but, despite his antics and startling contempt for conven-
tionality, he deserves to be encouraged as a bowler who
only wants a little ballast, as a batsman who has a
splendid “eye” and a slashing style. His electricity
delighted the crowd.
• • •
Trumble was temporarily disabled by influenza
during the N.S. W. v. Victoria cricket-match. When he
re appeared next day, weak and feverish, and they put
him on to bowl,umpire Flynn carried Hughie’s “sweater”
tied round his neck 1
# * •
Farcical scene at a cricket-match : Moses and
Callaway at the wickets, both limping on one leg, and
Donnan, who was running for the former, struck by a
leg-hit, joining in. Moses’ bad limb, however, outlasted
the others easily. Looked like a One-Legged XL v. Vic-
toria while it lasted.
• • *
In a desperate attempt to overtake a boundary-
hit when the Vies, were winning, Conningham very
nearly succeeded in impaling himself on the iron spikes.
After lying doubled up awhile, he crept shakily back to
his place with a dangling arm. His brilliant fielding
afterwards was loudly applauded.
• » *
A clever Sydney lawyer the other day bought a
horse lor £4O and straightway won £lOOO on him. Better
for that young man, in the long run, had he paid £lOOO
and won only £4O. His brain, as applied to his profes-
sion, is his best gold-mine. It’s the old story of the
storekeeper on the goldfield.

The Wild Cat Column.
Some Up-South smarties have just found out
that it doesn’t pay to be too clever. They have been
working a concern for a company, taking the by-proauots
for their remuneration and giving the company the prin-
cipal product free of cost. By working/or the by-products
they so reduced the output of the principal stun that tne
company couldn’t earn interest on its capital, so me
clever gentry got the whole concern at their own price.
Up went the output of the principal product and down
went the by-ones, till the thing was put into fine trim ipi
floating in England. They were just about scooping in
the dollars when- their game was knocked sky-nign
through the Englishmen getting hold of the returns toi
the previous five years. “For our purposes we must
have high-grade and not low-grade stuff,” was the cabled
consequence. And once more the keen-witted speculators
aye wondering whether straightforwardness doesn t give
the best pay-dirt after all.

A curiosity of the “ private composition ” boom
in Melbourne is the case of a man who, on a salary of a
few hundreds a year as a retail business manager, has
“ gone ” for a quarter of a million. Not every firm can
boast of a manager with a record like this. Another odd
circumstance is that nobody seems to understand how
the gentleman came to acquire his splendid liabilities, or
why his salary isn’t to be set aside for the next three
centuries for the benefit of creditors.

Dear Bulletin, —Your recent par re the Mid-
land syndicate may be true, but it is a notorious fact that
every business man in W.A. who has had anything to do
with it has been badly “ left.” Further, only recently
the Government had to advance £60,000 to keep things
moving, and now they are again approached with a
modest demand that they should guarantee interest on a
half-million loan to be raised in London. The Midland
may yet be the wreck-rock of the Forrest Ministry.
Westward Ho.

Dear Bulletin, —How difficult it is to get from
a bank-manager an outspoken answer to—what are you
charging for my overdraft? ‘‘Current rates, Mr. Im-
pertinent, current rates ; market very changeable and
the manager talks away until one rests assured the bank
is his godfather and his godmother and his affectionate
helpmate for life; yet his passbook after the balance
shows an amount that must be more than 71 per cent ,
average the overdraft and rates ever so high. It would
profit many a man to give his passbook to an accountant
and get the rate of interest charged discovered. I think
banks should be compelled to insert in all passbooks the
rate of interest charged, and a false statement be made
criminal.—Yours, 7J%? No, 10.
* * *
The Provident and Industrial Insurance Co., of
Maoriland, which has recently extended its business to
Sydney, is an amusing institution in its way. Its premium
income for the year ended 30th June, '92 was £4411, and
the working expenses £3575, or just about 81 per cent.,
and after paying claims—amounting to £77s—the glorious
total of £343 was added to the funds as the result of a
whole year’s work. The actuary “ sees no reason” why
a dividend of five per cent, should not be declared on the
paid up capital, and, so far as the layman s unin-
formed eye can estimate the situation, this will
absorb about £l5O, if the proposition is accepted,
and reduce the balance to a humble £193. The
company has been three years in existence, and has
been busy all that time issuing policies, and thereby
accumulating future liabilities, but apparently it takes
almost all its income to get the liabilities, and it hasn’t
time to save much to meet them at maturity. The total
funds in hand are £4515, of whicli nearly £3OOO are
capital, and about £1640 represents the “Assurance
Fund.” The industrial business -which is the ruinously
expensive variety of life-insurance —represents about
three-fourths of the company’s trade, and the rest is
ordinary life-insurance. As the all ■ round expenses
on both varieties of business are 81 per cent., this
seems to suggest that very nearly all the indus-
trial premiums go in exs. There are some weird
items among the assets: £1279 for “ extension of
business’’and an item of “ preliminary expenses ” look
like debts instead of assets to the uninstructed eye ; any-
how, if the co. winds up, The Bulletin is prepared to
swear that it can’t sell its “preliminary expenses” or the
costs of “ extension of business ” and pay a dividend out
of the proceeds. We don’t seem to hanker after an
interest in the Provident and Industrial Insurance Co. of
Maoriland.

shareholders elected.

“IT IS THREE THOUSAND MILES
LONG.”
We allude to the great Mississippi River in
America. For hundreds of miles of its course it
is yellow with the earth which the swift current
has torn from its banks . Probably the Missis-
sippi bears constantly in solution ground enough
to make many a rich farm, though the amount is,
of coarse, greater at times of flood. Thus, for
drinkifig, and even for washing, the water of the
Mississippi must first be purified.
It is the same with anything which either for
appearance or utility is in some way thrown out
of character. For instance', the blood in our body
is a river. From the heart to the extremities it is
of a bright red colour, but on its return through
the veins it is dark and sluggish. The reason is
that when it starts from the heart it has just been
purified, and when it comes back it is loaded with
all sorts of refuse matter it has picked up in its
journey. In other words, the blood—which is
only food fully digested—carries life on its red
current and brings away disease and death on its
d&fk one.
Speaking of her own condition at a period about
six years since, a woman writes that her skin
gradually became first yellow and then of a saffron
colour. Connected with this she experienced
several symptoms of illness. She had more or less
pain at the sides and considerable difficulty in
breathing. Her appetite, previously good, began
to fail, and after eating comparatively little, there
was much distress in the region of the stomach.
There was an attendant loss of energy, both of
mind and body, and she felt tired, languid, and
weak. .
Now we must observe that in case of natural
fatigue from labour or any exertion rest is an
unfailing remedy. Sleep and relaxation renew
our powers. But there is a kind of fatigue which
rest does not relieve—for which the Angel of
Sleep bears no healing on his wings. What vile
counterfeit is it ? , ,
She adds: “ I finally took to my bed and was
visited by a doctor, who said my liver was wrong,
and that I had jaundice. He gave me medicine,
but as it had no good effect he advised me to go
to the hospital. As I objected to this, he Baid i
might try a change of air and see what that would
do for me. So I gave up my place and went to
my home at Fairford, in Gloucestershire. I ail-
ing to obtain any benefit from the change, I con-
sulted another physician, who attended me for
some time, but I grew no better. Whatever I did
I could not recover my strength, and my friends
thought I was in a decline.
“I did not eat enough to feed a bird, and began
to despair of ever recovering my health. With
many ups and downs in the way of feeling, and of
fear and hope, I lingered along until July, 1890,
when an acquaintance recommended me to try
Mother SeigePs Syrup. Indeed, so much was I
impressed with what was said about this medicine
that I sent my niece four miles to procure a bottle
of it. After taking the contents I felt better. A
w'eight seemed to be lifted from my chest, my
relish for food returned, and by degrees I found
myself in every way stronger. I will conclude by
saving that after having used two bottles more of
the Syrup I was able to return to Birmingham
quite well, and have had no signs of the ailment
since. It is only just to mention that I feel better
now than I have done for many years, and had I
sooner known of Mother Seigel’s Syrup I should
have avoided an amount of suffering and misery
which it saddens me even to look back upon.”
The lady referred to is Miss Sarah Hawkes, of
the “Lion Inn,” Longmore-street, Birmingham.
That yellow or saffron colour of the blood was
caused by the failure of the liver to secrete, or
take away, the bile from the blood. Remaining
in the blood it was carried to all parts of the body,
and necessarily showed itself through the skin in
the ghastly hue she names. .
Understand, please, that the bile, useful to help
move the bowels, is a poison when in the blood.
The entire malady of biliousness in all its forms
is a symptom of indigestion and dyspepsia, the
source of most of our aches, pains, and diseases.
It is no new thing to say that the success of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup in reviving the torpid
digestion, stimulating the liver, and thus purify-
ing the blood, sets it apart from all other medicines
as an article to be relied upon when the use of
ordinary drugs lias been followed only by disap-
pointment. The blood holds the secret of life,
and this remedy keeps that ruby river clear and
clean.

Ballad of a Capitalist.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The charges that the vulgar herd
Again ,t us men of money bring
Are both unnatural and absurd,
And need but little answering :
So in your teeth this verse I fling,
And challenge you to find a flaw,
When I assert this sort of thing
Is in accord with natural law.
Why look : the little busy bee,
Of which the hymnal poets sing,
Unsexed, but quite content, we see,
Work bravely for its queen and king;
It lives for us illhstrating
That raven-Labour’s restive caw
Is wicked ; for its suffering
Is in accord with natural law.
We don’t unsex you ; thus we give
An advantage to the human thing ;
Take the concession—work and live
For us from whom such bounties spring.
Let’s hear no more of gathering
Riches for an “ insatiate maw ” :
Your state - although the truth may sting—
Is in accord with natural law.
L’Envoi.
Princes, accept this offering,
This proof which I first clearly saw ;
Misery and all that sort of thing
Is in accord with natural laic.
CHREMES.
Mrs. Besant has “sensated” England by
giving a Jesuit father, who sneered at the alleged
miracles of Theosophy, atu quoque. “What
about the Lourdes miracles and the Holy Coat at
Treves?” shrieked she. Just so. When creeds
fall out, the devil gets his own.
A husband advertises thus in a N.S.W.
country paper :
PUBLIC NOTICE.
TO ALL WHOM IT MAY CONCERN.
TVTY wife, , having left my home without
any just cause for complaint, I will not be respon-
sible for any debts contracted in my name by her or by
—r , Esq., the fellow in whose company she
went, together with six children. Trusting they will all
spend a Merry Xmas and Happy New Year, and will
stick to his bargain, I am, yours truly,
(Signed) William .
The husband was evidently unable to stick to his
bargain.
Dear Bulletin, —The Alice River Settle-
ment (C.Q.), now being run on Socialistic princi-
ples, was started by bootless “stonebrokers,” in
the worst aftermath of the shearers’ strike collapse.
Over 42 acres of heavy gidya-timbered country
have been cleared ; 33 acres are under cultivation;
several miles of stub-fencing have been completed;
a bakehouse, a kitchen, general dining-room, car-
penter’s shop, blacksmith’s shop, shoemaker’s shop
(all boots locally made), have been erected; also,
three cottages and two in course of erection. Some
of the men go to work outside and bring their money
back to the common fund ; every shilling is banked
as a reserve for food, tools, seeds, &c. ; and no
dividend can be declared for two years. Eight-
hour work is the principle, the work being
managed by elected heads of departments. They
recreate with cards, chess, draughts, &c., but lack
books. They have, been battling against a big
drought, the Alice being dry for months, which
necessitated the sending of the cattle and horses
30 miles for water. Yet their spring-cart goes
into Barcaldine every morning loaded up with
produce, and they are driving out the Chows.
They have a vineyard, also crops of potatoes,
maize, wheat, and oats. Lately, they obtained
the deeds of their land, five square miles, and
(says a letter) —“When our mates come home
with their cheques after shearing we mean to fence
in the block straight away. This will cost us
about <£2oo for wire alone; the timber and labour
we have on the ground.” There are only 32
members semaining, the balance not having had
the grit to wait longer for dividends. This only
represents 16 months’ start. What in 16 years ?
There are no bosses—all mates; no sack, no
strikers. —H.C.
The British Post-office, which the but-
toners of “our glorious constitution” generally
pretend is as “ tooralooral ” as that fraudulent
imposture, the Habeas Corpus Act —a noble pro-
vision which is invariably suspended whenever it
would do any service—is still “ Grahamised.”
The name is derived from the infamous Sir James
Graham, who, during the insurrectionary propa-
ganda by Mazzini to deliver Italy from the Aus-
trian yoke, intercepted correspondence from two
young Italian officers in the Austrian service to
Mazzini, then in exile in London, and surrendered
the letters to the Austrian Ambassador with the
result that the writers were hanged. Graham
pleaded that every British Ministry had habitually
violated the alleged secrecy of the Post-office.
Grahamising, we say, still goes on. “ The
police at Scotland Yard have examined
cipher communications describing the schemes.”
That is a recent cablegram relative to the
alleged anarchist plot. There is in the British
Foreign Office a secret cypher department of
experts, who are able, by familiarity with all cus-
tomary methods of secret writing, to interpret any
ordinary cypher. But there is one very simple
method of cypher communication which no export,
however capable, can unravel, although he will
know almost at a glance what the method is.
That is, for the parties to agree upon a book, each
having a copy, and to write just the numbers repre-
senting the page and line where each successive
word of their message occurs. Unless the
decypherist can hit upon the book, he is helpless.
Of course, the thing can be complicated by pre-
arranged substitution of one set of figures for
another, or of words or letters for figures, or, in
fact, by any number of such substitutions and per-
mutations. The skilled decypherist will quickly
unravel these accessories, but it would take him
fifty life-times, if he got no hint, to discover
which book among the millions published had
been chosen by the correspondents.

Some seven years ago a strange thing happened to the lady
whose photo, appears herewith, whereby she became
A. MYSTKRY
Which no one can explain or account for so far, not even her
husband. How it happened was this : She lay dying of a oomph*
cation of diseases ; often she would faint off, and lie unconscious
as long, as 30 hours at a stretch —frigid .as a corpse and apparently
dead. The leading medical men of Melbourne were in attendance,
often at a cost of Five Guineas a day, yet she sank lower and
lower. Everything that medical science could suggest was tried ;
. • * n ji _ ■ v. kniiva’ alpprt
IUVVCI. IjVCI J llllllg lIIIUV liivuivi* co . , .
even morphia failed to give enough ease for two hours sleep
daily. At last, in desperation, some said, “ Try Mes
' Mesmerism.”
Mr. Harle, as a Mesmerist,
Had been very successful on the public platform, and had also
saved life by private magnetic treatment; but Mrs. Harle, being
a lady of very pronounced opinions and strong mind of her own,
never could see anything in it hut a hobby of his, and so, when
urged to try it, she ridiculed the very idea ; hut they said, “ Try
it for sleep'only— you will go mad for want of rest.” For a long
time she could not bring her mind to submit; at last, after much
prayer to God for guidance and help, she was willing to try.
After about half an hour’s mesmeric influence and passes, she
went off into a peaceful hypnotic state, which lasted, as was
intended, from eight at night till ten next morning; and she
awoke rested and refreshed. The experiment was repeated, when
I»o! Everyone was Startled
To find that whilst to all intents and purposes she was asleep, yet in another sense, the mind was tar more avvaite,
and she commenced talking about what the servant was doing on the ground floor, what an acquaintance was doing a
mile awav what was happening in the home of a dear friend some few blocks away, and so forth. Then she was
requested’to look into her own body ; this she did, and w'as surprised herself, and surprised everyone else, by tracing
the cause of her trouble, which proved to be mainly an internal cancer. She then told them how to cure her.
Crowds of Friends Came to See Her,
For she had a large and influential circle of acquaintances. She astounded all by her marvellous powers of vision-
to see into their bodies as if they were made of transparent glass, to see the members of their families, to know all
about their commercial affairs, seemed quite easy to her ; England, America, New Zealand, etc., seemed just like next
door- The Sensation was Immense!
Drawing-room. Sitting-room, Dining-room and Bedroom were crowded with inquisitive people, and the invalid became
a phenomenon. The doctors were nonplussed with their patient-she gave directions how to soften the cancer to the
lancing state: this was done ; then, as they dare not give chlorof®rm on account of her heart, Mr. Harle put her undei
mesmerism, whilst Drs. Teague, of Collins Street, lanced it. She awoke without having felt anything that had hap-
pened. Everyone said: “ Start and use your powers for doing good, you can
Save Lives by Thousands.”
But her health was too low. First, she cured herself—in three months’ time she was again taking her accustomed
drives on the Esplanade, in her phaeton, to the astonishment of the whole district. To properly tone the system a
change of climate was needed, and she came to N.S. Wales. For a short time she resided in Maitland ; her retreat
w'as known by those who were aware of her powers, and she got no peace till she agreed to start practice, 1 ive years
ago Mrs. Hark opened in Newcastle ; three years out of the five have been spent travelling in the Northern district
and away in the bush discovering gold-mines, and she seems to be able to find gold as easy as trace disease.
Seven Thousand People Have Consulted Her
In her mesmeric state; some about business, but most about their health. Not one cao say : “lam worse for your
treatment ” whilst all who have followed the advice and treatment, rejoice in restored health. Mrs. Harle describes
each diseased organ, finds the cause of every trouble, and prescribes remedies and treatment. Her wonderful mental
vision or enables her to see just what medicine, and tow long it will take to cure it. often giving the
medicine three months ahead, and never changing a bottle.

The Meiamorphosis of Magindaus.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Nature had intended my neighbour, Andrew
Beal, for a scientist. Fate, and the decease of an
elder brother, made him the owner of a small
cattle-station. However, being smart and practical
enough, apart from his hobbies, he managed to
work the place profitably, and still find time to
pursue his experiments. His latest fad was the
reclamation of the aborigines by means of diet. I
used to listen patiently to his theories on the
subject; but to me, a blackfellow was simply a
blackfellow, he never could be any more, he never
could be anything else; and, moreover, he was
never intended to be anything else. lam quite
confirmed in that opinion now.
“Look at that fellow,” Beal would say, point-
ing to a young, lanky savage, with stupidity
written all over him. “In twelve months, under
a proper system of diet, I undertake to make him
the terror of the district.”
“Rot!” said I, impatiently.
“All right, but I’ll do it,” he returned savagely,
and walked back to the house.
Beal at once proceeded to act on his food theory.
What ungodly messes he fed that nigger on I
know not, but Magindaus (that was the nigger’s
heathen name) used to weep and protest bitterly,
and it was only by standing over him with a
pistol that Beal could at last induce him to
swallow some of the worst decoctions. There was,
however, no doubt about it; the nigger grew
abnormally strong and powerful. “As soon as he
is properly developed in body I will commence on
his brain,” Beal would remark.
Again I sneered. Magindaus was next door to
a blubbering idiot; whenever he was spoken to*
sharply, he used to sit down and cry. In a few
months under the dietary system he changed
entirely. He now stood six-feet-four and was
broad in proportion. If he became as violent in
his behaviour as he promised at present he cer-
tainly would be the terrorof thedistrict. Very soon
he began to assume an air of authority over my
friend to which the latter meekly submitted.
Frankenstein and his monster were nothing to it.
Mr. F. did at last “go for ” his monster.
All the men left the station, and Magindaus
insisted on the blacks camping in the house and
outbuildings and killing cattle whenever they felt
hungry. He made Beal eat all sorts of nigger’s
food, and stood over him with a nulla-nulla just
as Beal had stood over him with the revolver.
Finally, he insisted on his taking a gin to wife and
living like the other blacks.
Magindaus came over to my place once, but I
put the contents of a wire-cartridge into his
hinder hide and he retired. Then we held a con-
sultation as to the best course of action, for the
affair was becoming a public scandal. Two of us
being J.’sP. we issued a warrant against Magin-
daus and determined to ride over in a body and
order him to disperse in the Queen’s name. If he
refused we would capture him alive—or dead.
Magindaus was amusing himself with poor Beal
when we arrived there, and, when told to dis-
perse, commenced to use very bad language. Our
'measures were taken, however. Deaf Dick flung
a catching-rope neatly over his head, and Red Bob
■followed suit with another. Then we proceeded
to choke him down like a colt. But we made a
mistake, for Magindaus was not built like a colt,
and by the time we had him choked dowhi and
tied up Magindaus was dead.
We all looked rather foolish, and Beal, who had
grown quite imbecile, began crying grievously.
Whilst still gazing stupidly at each other I heard
a voice say :
■‘Now, bananas and fish, supplemented by a
generous meat diet, would alter the type in less
than two generations.”
Thank Heaven ! we were sitting on the veran-
dah, and Beal had sent me to sleep with one of
his long-winded speeches. .
“Do you intend to try the experiment I i
tasked*
“ I am thinking of it,” he answered.
“ Then take my advice and don’t, for I ve just
had such an awful dream.”
“ The devil you have ! Then we had better go
to bed.”
He has been very cool ever since.
FELIX SLOWBOY.
“The working-class that species of
slavery softened by a salary.”—Lamartine.
The late Lady Brassey used to carry a
marble figure of her dead child around the world
with her. There was always an eerie, peculiar
look in the unfortunate lady’s eye.
Kanakas, says Griffith, are imported for
cane-cultivation so that the poor white man may
get work. Api'opos, four able-bodied whites
lately took a contract to load cane for Bingera
(Bundaberg) Mill, on the cane-tramway at 6d. per
ton! They worked from sun-up till dark for 28
days, and earned £5 2s. 6d. —.£1 ss. 7id. per man.
And yet these ungrateful ruffians refused, after-
wards, to fall dense scrub, at =£l an acre, telling
the land-owner they were poor men enough, but
they wouldn’t like to rob him.
A Bundaberg (Q.) planter has been fined
<£2 for “ permitting ” a Kanaka to work inside a
sugar-mill. And this forty-shilling Nemesis is
duly telegraphed all over Australia to show people
how rigidly the Queensland Government carries
out its promises not to allow the nigger to do
work other than that for which he was imported.
But anyone who has lived on or near a Queensland
sugar plantation knows how the planter can
arrange such matters. The sugar-boilers and
engineers employed don’t care a rap if the entire
mill is run with Kanakas, provided that their
billets are not infringed upon, and even had they
the inclination to kick against rubbing shoulders
with a nigger they dare not do so. And as the
prosperity of small places like Bundaberg depends
upon the local sugar-mills no one but a rank out-
sider — an d these are kept at a distance during the
crushing season—would think of protesting, even
if he saw Kanaka sugar-boilers, engineers and
book-keepers. And so the planter feels secure,
and while Santo Jimmy, at 3s. a week, is plant-
ing the vegetables, Paddy from Tanna is making
bottle pap in the kitchen for the planter’s baby
who is out in his perambulator with another
nigger; meanwhile the planter’s wife is cursing
the stupidity of the ebon-coloured wench from
Malacca, who doesn’t all at once grasp the
intricacies of the family sewing-machine.

A Leg-Iron Song.
“The Dignity of Labour in Australia! Sleath and Fer-
guson and others, the cockatoo-jury-convicted Barrier
strike-leaders, shaved aud-oiopped and broad-arrowed and
leg-ironed and chained together two-and-two, have been
exhibited by the authorities on a railway-platform, on
route to their respective prisons.”
Rejoice, O Southern Nation, in darkness wandering
long !
Great Pluto reigns triumphant, and Might is
“ mighty strong ”;
To-day the glorious birthright our convict fathers
gave
Enthrones the God of Mammon and chains the
Barrier slave.
Clink, clink, clink.
The wild winds tell the story as they sweep round
Botany Bay,
It cometh from the Barrier, it’s cabled far away ;
The old Blue Mountain echoes send it on-towards
the sea,
And the tempests shriek in chorus the “Anthem
of the Free.”
Clink, clink, clink.
For this the God of Nature made Australia’s
' virgin soil,
For this we’ve fertilised it with tears and blood
and toil,
For this old Abel Tasman spread his white sails to
the breeze
That bore Australia’s Argonauts across the un-
known seas.
Clink, clink, clink.
The winds will tell the story, and the waves upon
the shore,,
For this Eureka miners dyed the great Stockade
with gore ;
For this the lips of brave old Lang hissed forth
the words of ii-e,
Till every burning sentence seemed writ in lines
of fire.
Clink, clink, clink,
O, the cash that’s spent on “ Government ” has
not been spent in vain,
For Shylock skins the people still with rack and
scourge and chain ;
Then swell the note of triumph till the wide world
hears afar —
“Three cheers for iron-fetters—for Might a wild
hurrah,”
Clink, clink, clink.
Under-Secretary Johnson, speaking at a
N.S. W. public school concert, said he once in-
spected a N.S. W. school where pigs congregated
under the floor, and interlarded his remarks with
approving grunts. The pigs stood it well till ho
reached “Object Lesson—Pigs,” when they re-
fused to hear themselves maligned and departed.
Tito Warnuvee (Melbourne) Club for
Women has been formally opened. “The Coun-
tess” was asked to “drink the first cup of tea,”
“ breathe the first slander,” or do whatever is the
equivalent for “turning the key,” “laying the
foundation-stone,” “ turning the first sod,” &c.,
but she declined, because some persons ”
expressed the vulgar desire to get a democrats
woman to do the job. The consequence was there
was no ceremony to speak of. There are about
351) members now, and the club is so far a success.
The “ladies” who at first seceded because the
members were called “ women ” have returned and
threaten to take charge of the club—which, by the
way, professes to exist in part to aid in the amelio-
ration of woman's lot. Yet it holds a sister in a
state of slavery for 12 hours a day. Its rnaid-of-
all-work, so says a correspondent, gets 15s. a week,
has to work on Sunday, and when she “asked
off” on Christmas Day, was growled at and
refused.
* G. R. Sims on the travelling Yankee :
There was another gentleman in the carriage who also
interested hie greatly. He was dark and dressed with
elaborate care, and he kept on producing instruments of
a remarkable character from his pockets. He was a man
who was evidently thoroughly equipped for all the con-
tingencies of everyday life. A blue bottle got into our
carriage, and he produced a fly-paper, on which it
promptly settled and died. He wanted to smoke,
and he took out a pipe with 17 distinct and separate
patents in it. Ho filled it from a pouch which
was absolutely remarkable for its ingenuity. He put
the tobacco in with a patent spoon, he rammed
it down with a metal stopper, then he put a skewer,
which he wore attached to a silver chain, up the stem,
then he slabbed the tobacco with a stiletto to make the
pipe draw more easily, and then he kindled a light by
means of an elaborate arrangement in six compartments,
which took about fifteen minutes to fit together. After
that he hung his hat up by a patent hat-suspender, put
on his gloves with a button-hook, squirted himself all
over with a scent spray, cut his newspaper with a paper-
cutter that was also a railway-key and a boot-jack, and
wrapped himself up in a patent rug which, by pulling
various strings, could be made into an overcoat, a mac-
intosh, a bed-quilt, a life-belt, a tablecloth and a shroud.
Finally, he went to sleep, after setting a pocket-alarm to
wake him up in an hour, and putting a patent catch on
his watch-chain, in case either of us should be taken
suddenly with kleptomania.
Editor Bulletin, — Me a par. of yours,
that a Maoriland man, just before the last elec-
tion in ’OO, paid £lOOO for one .£lO share in a
Christchurch (M.L.) paper, If the Conservative
one is meant there is a mistake somewhere. I
send you share-list of company-owners. You will
see by it that the nominal capital of the paper is
£OO,OOO divided into IfiOO deferred shares of £25
each, and 800 preferential shares of £25 each.
Shares not yet subscribed for, £7380. Of the
subscribed shares, the Landocracy own £34,500
worth. Law, £lO,OOO. This ought to be sufficient
to give the paper a decided tone in favour of land
monopoly and class legislation. To give it,
well, a sort of justice, it prints the same old
bunkum about the dangers of class legisla-
tion, “confidence is being destroyed,” “the
wage-fund is in danger,” “ progressive taxa-
tion is robbery,” &c., &c. One can read that
sort of article with his eyes shut. This paper, the
exponent of individual freedom and liberty, to be
consistent, has actually increased the tradediscounb
to 50 per cent. Thus is an eight-page newspaper
sold to the trade for a half-penny ! Not content
with this bait, it has also increased its Saturday’s
edition to twelve pages—and all for the same
price. The effect of this cut-throat competition
is to push its opponent, the Liberal paper, very
hard, and I have no doubt plenty of workingmen,
small farmers, and tradesmen, applaud such efforts
to give them good value for their pennies. As you
say, these people put their “pennies in the daily
paper slot ” and the returns are, “ insults,
poison.”—Yours, obediently, Tk Weka, Canter-
bury (M.L.).

THE ESTATE OF
FONTAINEBLEAU,
WENTWORTH FALLS.
rpms MAGNIFICENT ESTATE of 239 ACRES has been
surveyed by Messrs. Miller and Lewis, of Pitt Street,
Sydney, into 138 Allotments, ranging from 1 Acre
Residential Blocks to 10£ Acres, situate only 500 yards
from the WENTWORTH FALLS RAILWAY STATION,
on the opposite side of the line. It embraces all that fine
and elevated land opposite the mansion of R. M. Pitt,
Esq., whose splendid garden excites the admiration of
every passenger on the Great Western railway.
When the delightful climate of Wentworth Falls is taken
into consideration, the place may be safely described as
The Madeira of the Bine Mountains.
Delicate constitutions and worn-out business men are
benefited equally by the refreshing purity of the 'mountain
air, and the estate is not surpassed for residential purposes.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE
is well described by the poet, viz.:
The waralah, all gleaming red,
Lifting in pride its stately head,
Shines through the clustering ferns ;
While as the wattle’s yellow bloom
Falls in soft showers, its deep perfume
The air to sweetness turns.
Would you this haunt of beauty know?
'Tis the charming Estate of Fontainebleau !
The Blaxland Road is now being made from the Railway
fence, and, as it runs right through this magnificent estate,
it will, when finished, be without exception the finest
Carriage Drive on the Mountains.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE is the most elevated
and beautiful about Wentworth Falls - far and away
superior to the land on the other sijje of the line. The
picturesque views and’ the extreme fertility of the soil
renders the place very attractive, and specially adapted
for Fruit or Flower Gardens, Orchards or Dairy Farms,
for which Nature seemsto have intended it.
